O, my sons, now that I have completed this book, which is the last of the sciences of medicine, by completing its purpose at the end and through its explanations and clarifications, it seems to me that I have made clear to you the treatise which concerns what I do myself, namely surgery.
For surgery is completely neglected in our region and in our time, to the point that the practice of it has almost disappeared and its knowledge remains scarce; we only find small descriptions of it in the books of the Ancients, which have altered much of it, leading people into error and hesitation because they do not understand its principles and thus weaken its power and art.
And it seems to me that I should revive this treatise according to the order of exposition and clarification and abbreviation; and so that it comes with the forms of cautery tools and other instruments for the work as mentioned by adding explanations and preparation, which cause is necessary.
But the reason why a good practitioner is not readily found in our profession at this time is that the art of medicine is extensive, and one must be well exercised in the science of anatomy, which involves knowing the functions of the members and their forms, as well as their connections and separations; understanding the bones, nerves, and muscles, their numbers and distributions; and the pulsating and non-pulsating veins, along with their origins.
And for this reason Hippocrates said that in name there may be many, but few in actual practice, especially in the art of hands.
This may we have said about that at the introduction of this book; for who is not learned in what we have said about anatomy, is not exempt from falling into error through which men are made to die, as I have seen too many of those, for some are formed in this science and throw themselves from that lack of science and lack of experience.
For I saw a foolish doctor who had incised an abscess in the neck of a woman and cut some arteries of the neck, so much blood flowed that the woman died shortly thereafter.
And I saw another doctor who attempted to remove a stone from a man who was ill, and the stone was large, and he came to him and pulled out that stone along with a piece of the bladder, whereupon that man died on the third day.
And I had already called out to avoid removing that stone, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I recognized that he would die.
And I saw another doctor who was receiving payment from a duke of our land for surgery, and he attended to a black castrated man who had a fracture in his thigh; and because of the doctor's ignorance, he exposed the fracture over the wound with splints and sticks in a strong structure, but did not leave the wound breathing.
And he loosened it according to his desires, and then bandaged it, and after a few days commanded us to loosen the bandage. And thus between the time of loosening and rebandaging, the thigh and leg became abscessed, and he came to ruin.
And he calls out to him, and he prepares to untie the ligature, and when calm is achieved he begins to alleviate his pains.
But when corruption was confirmed in the limb, so that it could not be restrained, and the corruption did not cease to spread throughout the limb until the patient was in danger.
And I saw another surgeon who incised a cancerous abscess, and it became ulcerated within a few days, to the extent that it greatly revealed the skill of his lord.
The reason the malignant cancer is clear of melancholic humor is that it does not need to reach the heart entirely, but rather remains in the member which can tolerate being completely affected by it.
And for this reason, children, it is necessary for you that the operation be devised in two aspects: one is an operation associated with health, and the other is a work involving great risks.
And I have already stated this in every part of this book where there is an operation involving error and fear; therefore, it is necessary for you to guard against that and leave aside this, so that the pages do not find a way to speak falsely and dishonor you.
And so take up your weapons with diligence and the protection of God, and may your patients be with you in fortune and steadfastness.
And use the best path by the grace of God to lead to health and praiseworthy success, and avoid terrible diseases that are difficult to heal, and remain armed against what causes you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and reputation does not evade you: for it remains more for your glory, and less in this world and later in regard to your sanctity.
Wound treatment in some monastic surgeries: Do not treat illness badly, for thus bad doctors are not named.
And I have dedicated this book according to three chapters.
Begin the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter is ordered about the head and neck, in the forms of the instruments and in the tools of the cautery, and of what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, about lancing and puncturing, and about cupping, and plasters, and the extraction of arrows and similar items, all chaptered and ordered regarding the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, about restoration and dislocation, and the treatment of twists and similar conditions; a chapter ordered concerning the head and neck, and the forms of the instruments.
I shall speak of the quality of the movements of that cautery and of its ingredients, and in what condition cautery is prohibited.
I say that the discourse on the quality of the movement of cautery and its ingredients is long and extensive, subtle by science, and hidden as a secret; and many wise people have spoken about it, and they disagree among themselves. It suffices for me to mention a little of their discourses, fearing lengthiness.
Therefore, I say that cauteries conform to all complete configurations universally, whether they are made of matter or without matter, except for two configurations, which are tail-like configurations without matter.
In the tail-like and dry configuration made of matter, one should become cautious; for some say that cautery is helpful in such cases, while others argue the opposite, stating that cautery is not appropriate for diseases caused by heat and dryness, since the nature of fire is hot and dry, and it would be inappropriate to treat a tail-like and dry disease with something hot and dry.
And let us say that those who argue the opposite are mistaken, because cautery is beneficial in all tail-like and dry diseases that occur in human bodies. For when you compare the body of a person and its humidity to the nature of fire, you find that the human body tends to be more fragile.
And I say according to the words of that person, that experience has already shown this to me on several occasions.
But it does not befit one who has already been trained and exercised in the chapter of cauteries through previous practice, and who knows the diversity of human configuration and the disposition of diseases in their contexts, as well as their causes and accidents, and the time span of these matters.
But in other configurations, there is no need for fear on your part, especially in fragile and humid diseases, since all physicians are already in agreement about those, and they do not disagree regarding the use of cautery in such cases.
And you know, my students, that the secret of healing with fire and its effectiveness lies in the fact that fire is simple, and its action affects only the part being cauterized without harming other parts of the body nearby, causing only minor discomfort.
Here is the translation:
Take the fire, by its sublimity and by the goodness of its substance, it does nothing but superfluify [evaporate]. And it is known to us through experiment and through length of service in studying the art about the truth of the causes; and thereby I excuse myself from the length of speech. And if not that it were not read in my book in this pausero [pause] to you secrets hidden with the fire, and the quality of the operation of it on the bodies and of its expulsion by which you expel diseases, with philosophical demonstrative speech, which would be sharp in your possessions.
And know, children, that they err who do cauteries at times which are not appropriate, and they miss the best time of spring.
And I say that cauteries are appropriate at all times, because the harm caused by accidental time is submerged in the benefit drawn from what the cauterization attracts, and even more so if the cauterization is for pains or necessary pain, strength and boils, which do not tolerate delay due to the fear of malice coming from those that enter through improper timing.
And this does not concern your possessions, or, children, what is valued only by the common people and foolish doctors is that for the disease which is healed by cauteries, it is not always timely, and they miss that consequence.
And the cause is not as they think, for cauteries are nothing but a substitute for medicine that alters the complexion and dries up the humidity which is the cause of pains.
But still, cauteries are superior in medicine due to the speed of their effectiveness and the virtue of their operation and the greatness of their power.
It is possible that his illness may return at some time according to the condition of the patient and the location of his illness, and its nature, and what is prepared in him from the accumulation of superfluities within him, and what has reached him through his acquisition in food and similar causes; but if the illness for which cautery was administered is a subtle one involving few superfluities and humidities, such as the cautery of a tooth for pain and the like, it may not return that pain, but this happens rarely.
And the common saying is that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, and it is the saying of craftsmen, not because those who understand it think there is no cure in medicine or any other cause before cautery, but because they believe that once cautery is necessary, it is the most effective treatment.
And the cause is contrary to that; and the understanding that cautery is the last remedy of medicine is only when we administer methods of treatment for certain illnesses, and those medicines do not suffice, and then at the end of the process, cautery is administered, and it proves effective. And here at this point it is said that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, not according to the understanding which I grasp of the common and excessive practices of foolish doctors.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except for the equality of gold and its superior quality in substance.
And they said that the place for cautery does not matter, which is absolutely not true, because I have seen it proven and corrected that this works only on certain bodies and not others. Cautery with gold can be better than with iron, as they say; however, when you use fire with gold, it is not clearly defined, since the hardness of the gold affects how much heat it can withstand, and it prepares to receive friction. If you adjust the heat, the gold will melt due to the fire, and if you place some sheets in the fire, the craftsman can work on them during this process.
And thus it is done with cautery made of iron, you should remove the pus gently and gradually according to the correctness of the procedure.
And I have already arranged this chapter on cautery according to the chapters, and I have arranged those of the head among the priorities, so that I may relieve the seeker of what they ask for.
The first chapter.
This cautery is applied to the humidity and coldness of an accidental ailment caused by these two causes, which are the cause of rheumatism and various types of flu, called corissa, affecting the head and parts of the ears with fluid discharge, excessive sleep, toothache, and throat pain, and generally all diseases that come from coldness, such as paralysis, stroke, and similar conditions in those illnesses.
The form of this cautery is as follows: First, command the patient to evacuate with laxative medicine for the head purification, for three or four nights, depending on the strength of the patient and their age and habits.
And then command him to shave his head with a razor, and then place him seated on a padded bench and have him rest his head on his hands.
And then place the palm of your hand near the nose on the shaved area between his eyes. But when the temperature of your hand has reached it, mark that spot with a needle, and then ignite the olive wood cautery, which is made as follows:
And after that pause, place it on the marked spot, in such a way that it descends, with which the skin may be gradually stretched, and you rotate it.
And then lift your hand gently and look at the spot, and if you see any quantity of bone already exposed, like the amount of wheat grains, then raise your hand, and if not return your hand to that tool or another of those, if it is chilled, until you can see the bone as I have told you.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the spot and let it be for three days.
And then place it on the wound enclosed in leather and let it be inside until the burn scar is destroyed. And then treat the spot with a quadruple ointment until it is healed.
And they say that when the plague has opened up, the arm recovers better and more quickly.
And some of them say that the wound should be cauterized down to the bone, and the cautery should be kept until it adheres to some part of the bone.
And then remove what is adhered to the bone, and then it should be treated.
And others say that with the cautery reach to the deepest part, until it presses strongly on the bone until the bone melts, and this is the method of treating small wounds. And they consider that vapors from the head expire from that place.
And let the wound be treated for a long time until it is healed and has consolidated.
But I have not seen these two species of cautery in all cases, but only on some people out of fear; and leave that one, if you please, for it is better to preserve health when possible; for the head becomes inflamed by the solution of continuity as we have testified in other members, and especially when the constitution of the patient is cold.
And the first species of cautery is safer and better in my opinion; and with that one you can preserve health and well-being.
Chapter Second.
On Cautery of the Head.
When chronic and long-lasting pain occurs throughout the head and this is prolonged in the patient, even after using herbs, cloths, compresses, plasters, poultices, cats, hens, and similar treatments, especially if it has already been cauterized with a cautery which we have described but without any benefit from those causes, then consider whether the constitution of the patient’s head is strong in nature; and if it is not cold and shows no great sensitivity, then proceed to cauterize with another type of cautery below that one slightly. And then cauterize that area on the crown of their head with a cautery until the pain is alleviated. Cut open the affected area and expose the bone in the quantity that we have described, and cauterize it with a cautery at the back of the head in the place called "vas," — which is the location where the windpipe is situated. And be careful here and do not expose the bone, for the patient will suffer great pain different from the pain of all other cauteries of the head.
And here I will describe this cautery in its place, for it is appropriate that the cautery with which you cauterize the horns of the head and the occiput is a finer cautery than that with which the middle part of the head is cauterized; and this is the form of it.
Chapter Three.
On Cauterization of Non-Ancient Hemorrhoids.
When severe pain occurs in the head region or persistent pain inside the skull, it should be evacuated with medicines that purify the head, and other treatment should be administered as I have described for various diseases; and if this does not improve the condition, cauterization is then performed in two ways: either cauterization with an acute combustible medicine, or with iron.
But if you perform cauterization with a combustible medicine, then take a piece of cotton wool, soak it in the medication, and tie off both ends. And then incise the area of the pain on the temple with a lancet, cutting just deep enough to reach beneath the skin, so that it is hidden.
And then apply pressure to it with a bandage in a moderate compression, and leave it like that for three hours; then loosen it and remove the cotton wool, and let the wound be treated for two or three days, after which press upon it with a piece of cloth soaked in butter until the area becomes warm.
Therefore, take care of that wound with dressing so that it may heal.
And if you wish, apply some medicines that burn, of which I have written in the second treatise on burning medicines.
But when you use cauterization with this method, here is the fire for cauterizing, of which this is the form:
and it is called a nail clamp, because its head is in the shape of a nail, which has some curvature, and at the middle part of it there is a small prominence.
And then press on the location of the pain, and hold your hand—so that the artery below is not adhered—and move the tool gently little by little with speed.
And if the amount of it that adheres due to the specificity of the condition is such that half of it is adhered, raise your hand so that the artery below is not adhered, so that there is no bleeding.
And then soak cotton in salt water and place it on the location, and leave it for three days; and then apply dry cotton with borders, and afterward take care of the wound with dressing so that it may heal.
And if you wish, cauterize at the end of the radial bone according to the cautery method, and guard against cutting the artery in this proper but non-traditional procedure.
Chapter four.
On the cauterization of an ancient ulcer.
When you treat an ulcer as we have described in the previous treatment and in the divisions of diseases, and if it does not improve, and sometimes due to the disease, what we have mentioned about the first cautery with medicine or the cautery with fire is insufficient, then it is necessary for you to ignite the radial cautery until it becomes white-hot. Place it on the location of the pain along a line as long as half your finger or slightly more; and then move your hand once and press firmly so that it does not adhere to the artery but penetrates into the bone.
However, you must be careful about the continuity of the mandible, which is necessary for chewing, and ensure that the muscle is not adhered to and the nerves which move it are not affected, and that a spasm does not occur.
And if it is about the care and caution regarding the flow of blood from the artery that is being tied off, because there is fear in doing so, especially for one who does not know what they are doing and is not diligent and experienced; and leave the operation to someone else at the beginning.
And here I will come to the recall of the regimen for controlling blood flow in cases involving arteries according to the usual method, in its proper place in this book.
And if you see that this disease does not respond to these cauteries and the patient's condition is tolerable, cauterize in the appropriate place of the head as we have recounted, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you perform the cauteries that we have mentioned in the chapter on the rupture of arteries with a cautery having two edges, this is a better and more effective cautery.

Chapter VI.
On the cautery of torticollis.
Torticollis, which is cured by cautery, is not caused by any specific type of phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Cautery is avoided for the type that arises from dryness or spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of torticollis with jars and capipurgi and guargarisms, and if your treatment does not confere (succeed), then it is necessary for you to cauterize the disease with three cauteries: the first should be at the root of the ear, and the second a little away from the temple, and the third should be at the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the affected part, because softening occurs only in the part that appears healthy.
The method of cautery is that you cauterize it with the cautery at the side of the uppermost part of the ear, a little above the horn of the head, and on the temple another, whose length should be according to the length of the middle finger, and then place your hand with the cautery until half the quantity of the root is burned.
This is the form of cautery, and it is specific to the scissor-like instrument, the form of which is laid out above. However, it is even more subtle that there is a small amount as you see; and it is necessary to use a knife instead of excessive coarseness in this small amount.
And properly care for the area with water that has been previously processed until it is healed.
Chapter VII.
On the cautery treatment for chronic apoplexy.
When apoplexy is chronic and you treat it with water as we have said and do not induce fever through your medication, then cauterize the area using four cauteries: on top of all parts of the head; and at the point where we have mentioned, in the middle of the head; and one cautery on the nape of the neck. And the method of the cautery is done as mentioned above.
And it has already been cauterized over the mouth of the stomach; and this is better; and now it should be treated with water that you have prepared.

On the cautery treatment for forgetfulness performed with flame.
It is necessary to give the patient large doses and pills at the beginning to purify and cleanse them.

In some cases, cautery should be used, for that is the method of treatment. Do this according to the order which I have already mentioned.

And after each cauterization, wait until the swelling of the finger subsides, and then proceed with the next cauterization.
But if you prefer and the condition allows it, then apply the cautery in a moderate manner; and if you wish for a stronger effect, then apply it over both sides.
Then proceed with the care to ensure it heals; and let the cautery be olive-shaped as described above.
Chapter IX.
On the cautery of parasites and the softening of the entire body.
Proceed with the purification of the head using shears and as we have discussed; then shave the patient's head, and afterward apply cautery in a moderate location on the head, over all parts of the head, over the rear part of it, and with three cauterizations over the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary for the illness of softening the body to do so and the patient can tolerate it and the illness is severe, then apply four cauterizations over the vertebrae of the back, and finally apply one cautery deep into the area below the armpit, and raise the skin slightly. For the treatment of that which should be healed, use this; and for that which should be cauterized, use olive.
Chapter X.
On the cautery for epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized unless the epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And so it is necessary to modify the brain of the patient at the beginning of the year and follow the other treatment that we have described in the division when the patient is strong and can tolerate the exception of medicines.
And if it is a child who cannot tolerate medicines, then administer gentle treatments and food that purifies the brain, for several days before this, along with the improvement of diet.
And then shave the patient's head, and afterwards cauterize one area in the middle of the head according to the previous method, and with another cauterization at the occiput, and on each side of the head as well. And if the patient is strong and can tolerate it, then cauterize as I have described, with paralysis and relaxation of the body over the cervical vertebrae and the thoracic vertebrae.
And use olive cauterization as was previously mentioned.
But if the patient is a child, then use gentle cauterization according to this method.
Chapter XI.
On the cautery for melancholia.
When the causes of melancholia are corrupted humidity and thick phlegm, then cauterize that part with the cauteries which we have mentioned in the case of paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholia is excess leaning towards melancholy, and the body of the patient is humid, then give him to drink what purifies his brain as was mentioned earlier according to the division.
And then shave the head of the patient, and then make a poultice properly prepared over the circle, and then place it in the middle part of the head.
And the patient should be seated, and held from all sides.
And then apply a liniment; it is more suitable to use before sheep's wool. And warm that on the fire with moderate heating as needed, which the patient can tolerate when it is applied. And then place that in the middle part of the head in the circle, and let it be rubbed there.
And do this for the patient once a week, following another regimen until he is healed; or if you prefer, cauterize that in small points as needed, but have your hand guided by a properly ordered cautery: this is a special type of cautery which moistens the brain with equilibrium.
And then place that paste in cloth or in linens of chicken feathers.
Chapter XII.
On the cauterization of water descending in the belly.
When you have identified the beginning of water in the urine by the signs that I mentioned in the division, then start giving the sick person to drink water that cleanses the head, and guard him from all humidity.
And make him sweat in baths for several days.
And then command him to shave his head, and cauterize that area with one cautery in the middle of the head; and then cauterize that area with two cauteries on both temples if it is the beginning of water appearing in two urines, or on one side if it is the beginning of water appearing in one urine.
Inciase with one cautery all the veins and arteries that you see near the temples; and place the cauteries at a distance in the latitude of the temples.
And guard against bleeding when he is cauterized; and if you see him bleeding, stop the bleeding with some medicine that is available to you.
And I will come to the knowledge that is in the extraction of arteries and the ligation of them and the caution against bleeding.
And sometimes it is cauterized on the neck near two bones with two cauteries placed backward, producing a result.
Chapter XIII.
On the cauterization of old tears.
When tears from the eyes are old and constant, passing through veins and arteries that are visible on the surface of the head, and it is verified that this is cold phlegmatic excess, then cauterize in one place as we have recounted in the chapter on the beginning of water accumulating in the urine, and with one cautery in the middle of the head, and with two cauteries in the neck near two temples, and with two cauteries in the neck near two bones. And if you need an addition, then cauterize that at the full length of the nail of the finger on the extremity of the eyebrow with a small cautery.

On Cauterization for Nasal Discharge.

And be careful with the cauterization so that you do not burn it excessively.
And this is the form of the carnation-sized cautery.
Chapter .xv.
On Cauterization for Softening the Eyelids.
When the eyelid is softened due to illness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with this lunar-shaped cautery; and this is the form of it:

Chapter XVI.
On using cautery on the eyelid when the hairs have turned inward and are piercing the eye.
When the hairs of the eyelid turn inward and pierce the eye, cautery is applied in two ways: either by using an instrument or by applying a combustible medicine.
If cautery is done with fire, instruct the patient beforehand to trim their eyelashes as those of people in general, making them longer and even; and if they are piercing due to their growth from the root, then bandage the eyes so that nothing enters until the new lashes grow.
When they have grown and become even, then instruct the patient to place their head in your lap, and gently apply a bandage over the eyelids, shaped like a maple leaf, with the beginning of the bandage near the lashes.
And gently place a piece of boiled hen's feather or mucilage of silk over the eye.
And then perform the cautery according to this method.
And then cauterize over the shape you have marked, little by little, in small strokes, until the surface of the area shaped like a myrtle leaf is burned thoroughly and clearly.
A sign of health in this work is that you see the eyelid already contracted and the hairs raised around the piercing of the eye.
And thus raise your hand and wash it for three days, and then place it on the wound edge until the scar is healed. And then take care of that until it is healed.
But if the hair returns due to some cause and the eyelid is relaxed, now perform the cautery on that again just as you did initially.
But if the hair remains on the eyelid below, then cauterize that until it is raised to its natural place and healed so that the hair does not touch the eye.
But the cautery and medicinal burning are such that you command the patient to pluck the hair until it is lengthened and healed, and then make from paper or cloth the shape of a myrtle leaf.
And then take known soap and one ounce of live caus (likely a typo or archaic term), and mix these causes well, and apply it quickly so that it is not rubbed; then spread this over the paper made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place it on both eyes; and place a cloth-covered bandage under the eye, and the patient's head should be in your lap.
And place your finger on the medicine and press it slightly so that the patient feels the sensation of the medicine, for this sensation is like being burned by fire.
And thus, when the swelling and biting ceases, leave off the medicine and wash with the water you mentioned; then once the biting stops, resume washing the wound with water. Then consider whether the eyelid has already risen as it was washed; if so, bind it with heat or incision because that is better. And if not, go back to applying the medicine in the place where no medicine has been applied and do not let it darken until your operation is well done and the wound contracted.
And then pause on the wound at the edge until the trace of the adjustment is eradicated, and then treat that with definite definition or another method until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you to guard with diligent vigilance that no part of the medicine falls into the wound. And if after some days the eyelid becomes softened and any cause for hair grows in the wound, then reapply your medicine to the place where the eyelid has become softened, and properly as you did at the beginning, and then wait until it is healed.
And this is the form of the leaf of myrtle.
And know that the wounds of people vary in size and magnitude, and therefore according to this, you adjust your treatment.
And the way of rectitude will not be hidden from one who studies in this art.
Chapter XVII.
On the cautery treatment of a fistula that is in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat fistula with the water that we have mentioned in the sections on diseases and do not confer your treatment, then it is necessary that you cauterize it according to this method: command the patient to place his head or thigh in your hand, and hold the head of a cautery between your hands but in such a way that it is neither too much nor agitated near the head.
And place some cotton in an eggshell or in a powder of petals on the wound.
And then heat the cautery which has this form:
which is hollow, as it has the shape of a reed or eagle's feather, with one end through which the cautery is made.
And if you wish, it can penetrate into the other end. And if you want it to be as thin as a thread.
But the one that is hollow is better for your work.
And then press the fistula if it is open, and let some of the pus come out and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the pus from it.
And then place the cautery, which should be well heated, on it, and hold it there for a moment but not too long, so that you do not burn the wound or disturb the patient, and then place the cautery on the edges of the wound and cauterize it.
And if at the beginning of your cauterization it penetrates to the bone, that is good; but if not, then repeat the cauterization once or twice if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, and then place some cotton on it and around it with a drying ointment until it is healed.
If after ten days it has not healed, then at that time apply acute corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed, according to what is convenient in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula, which the ancients first described: enter into the location of the fistula and make an incision in it; then apply to that fissure finely powdered material made from its form. And pour into it one ounce of molten lead; hold your hand gently but firmly so that the pus does not come out where the molten lead reaches; and you must place over the wound cotton soaked in egg white or water, because lead adheres to the location of the fistula and heals it miraculously.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said about cauterization and treatment, it is good; but if not, then it is necessary to administer traction through the nose and reduce the fistula to the body of the nose as it will come into its proper place.
Chapter XVIII.
On the cauterization of the cleft of the buttocks.

Chapter XIX.
On the cautery treatment for a fistula in the mouth.
When there is an abscess in the uvula or on the palate or at the roots of the teeth below, and it causes pain, and then it breaks, and becomes old, and pus flows and a fistula is formed, and when you treat that one and it does not improve with treatment, now you must ignite a cautery according to the amount received in the fistula, and then place that heated part into the track of the fistula, and do this so that the fire reaches the beginning of it; and at last do this once or twice. And afterwards treat it as we have said for internal treatment until it is healed.
And if the fistula is severed and heals, that is good; but if not, then it is necessary to expose the location and remove the corrupted bone as will be described in its chapter.
Chapter XX.
On the cautery treatment of teeth and on softening the gums.
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth are unhealthy, and you treat them with medicines but without success, now place the tip of the malatue (a tool) in your mouth, and then ignite the cautery whose form will be described shortly. Place the quill (a thin rod or tube) over the tooth and insert the ignited cautery into it quickly, and hold it with your hand for a short time until the heat from the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
Then take your hand away, and then repeat the cauteries as often as you wish.
Then the patient should rinse his mouth with salt water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out, for the bad tooth is hardened and the softened gum is strengthened and the corrupted moisture is dried up.
Chapter XXI.
On the cautery for toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness, or there is decay or worm damage, and if you do not wish to treat it with medicine, then the cautery should be applied in two ways: either with a cow's horn or with fire.
Cautery with a horn is when you take a cow's horn and heat it over charcoal, then take cotton and wrap it around the tip of the horn, and then immerse that heated horn in the hot horn until it is warm, and place it quickly on the painful tooth before it cools.
Then repeat that several times until the effect of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, soak wool or cotton in the aforementioned horn and place it on the painful tooth, and then apply heated iron to it until the heat reaches the source of the pain in the tooth.
However, when using cautery with fire, you should attend to the length of the tube made of lead or iron, and it should be of such a size that the intensity of the heat does not reach the patient's mouth.

Chapter XXII.
On Cauterization of Hemorrhoids.
When hemorrhoids are full of phlegm and humors that resist digestion despite medication and you wish to aid their digestion gently, then you heat the cautery according to the form of which is described below. And if the hemorrhoid is small, then place the cautery according to the size of the hemorrhoid, and after that leave it for three days, then place it on the wound in saliva until what the fire has burned is healed.
And then take care of it with applications, bandages, and dressings until it is healed.

On cautery for sore throats and on the constriction of veins.
When the humors from the veins flow into the channel of the lungs, and especially when this is due to cold complexion, it is necessary that the patient be evacuated at the beginning with laxative medicines, and then cauterize that area near the root of the throat.

And then treat that area with what has been prescribed until it is healed.

Chapter XXVII.
On the cautery of an inflamed testicle.

Chapter XXVIII.
On the Incision of an Abscess of the Testicle with Cauterization.
When you find an inflammation or abscess in the testicle and want to know if that abscess is in the flesh of the testicle or in the tunica, then if it is in the flesh of the testicle, the patient will show heaviness and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the tunica of the testicle, then the pain will be sharp and often exhausts the physician's ability to treat it.
Therefore, the patient should be positioned on his back; then approach to feel the location of the abscess; and then apply the cautery which resembles a reed, the form of which was described on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until the entire area is consumed, until it reaches the cicatrix, until it causes scabbing.
And then treat that as you would for the treatment of other abscesses until it is healed.
And it is not appropriate to use this form of cautery except for those whose study in the art of medicine is extensive and who have known many diseases; but even then, one should only attempt this experiment a few times before relying on it, and it is better to be cautious.

Chapter XXX.
On the cautery of the ulcer.
When you treat the disease of the ulcer as we have said about treatment in division and do not combine your cure, then there are three ways to apply the cautery, all of which are effective. And there should be a space between each cautery of the width of a finger or a little more, and the method of the cautery should be the same method that we have described for the cauterization of the testicle.
And do not apply your hand to the cautery; and let the patient lie on their back.
And there is another method of cauterizing, which is to ignite the cautery with two vertical lines, as we have described in the chapter on the dislocation of the muscle, and elevate the side where the ulcer is located, so that it receives the weight of the patient's left side.
And so raise it as high as you do when you lift the chain according to the length of the body, so that cautery may be done according to the width of the body.
And then insert the two ends, which have been ignited, into the opening where the chain penetrates from the other side.
And then remove the cautery; and thus four cauteries have been performed.
And if you wish, cauterize with a cautery that has three ends.
And then take care of the cautery so that it does not shake or move for too many days, for it is more convenient than the other causes previously mentioned in the treatment.

On the cautery of the artery near the removal of the tooth.

On the cautery of fistulas that are formed in the nature of the flesh in various parts of the body.

And then apply the cautery to those corrupt parts until they are consumed completely in one application or two or three, as your need requires.

And then when you want to lift him after forty days, administer it carefully and have him remain for another forty days.

And I will speak about the treatment of ruptures in other parts of the abdomen, and at the beginning, you should not delay, but cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and take what is excised from the wound as we have said, one-third of it; and then treat that part as we have described, and there is no adjustment necessary.
And some earlier authors said that the rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery in this form, as it is marked with a template:
and draw a line that you follow along the latitude above the navel, and another line above it; then place your hand with a nail-shaped cautery, but the first cautery is faster and better.
Chapter XLVI.
On the cautery treatment for torsion.
When you find in some members due to falling or impact that there is pain and this pain persists for a long time and does not improve with medication, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cautery for each member be according to its size and smallness, its strength and weakness, and the location of the torsion and pain.
If it heals with the first cautery, that is good; but if not, then repeat the cautery on it. For it is customary for such pains to be punctured in a member near where they occur, so you must achieve this with the cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter XLVII.
On Cauterization for Leprosy.
In some cases, leprosy can be treated effectively with cauterization, but it is less effective for the type of leprosy that arises from the putrefaction of flesh and melancholy.
And therefore, when you wish to cauterize that person, consider whether the leprosy is contagious and if it can be treated with what we have discussed in the division and does not exist elsewhere. If you fear that the corruption may spread throughout their body and entire composition, then cauterize them on the head with a hot iron: place one at the middle of the head, and another further down near the forehead; two on both temples; and one at the back, on the nape or neck.
Finally, place the cauterization a little deeper so that it slightly presses into the bone, causing the affected layers of skin to be displaced, thus easing the expulsion of thick vapors; and cauterize over the area as needed.
And if the leprosy has already spread over the body and is clearly manifest, then you should cauterize those areas with the irons we have mentioned for the head; and cauterize one spot at the end of the nose, two spots on each cheek, two on both sides of the neck vertebrae, six on the back vertebrae, a large one near the hip bones, one on each hip bone, two on each thigh, one on each leg, two on each elbow and one on each wrist, two on each knee, two on each muscle, and two on each tendon of the feet; cauterize all the joints of the fingers and toes, as well as each heel, and the armpits.
And guard against the nerves which are above the heel so that you do not burn them.
And sometimes cautery is applied with one cautery on the bone of the pubis, and another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the spleen.
And know that the more you adjust the cauteries, the more beneficial and effective they are; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery as the others do, because the body of the patient is already numb.
And it is appropriate that the cauteries be large or few according to the members and joints, depending on the properties of the tools.
And apply the cautery with flour of barley mixed with honey, and continue the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter XLVIII.
On Cauterization for Inducing Stupor.
When a member is numb or asleep and is treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and does not heal, then you should cauterize that numb member with cauteries according to the size of the member or its smallness.
And let the heat of the cautery enter the depth of the skin a little. And then treat that area with an ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes numbness in some part of the body, whether it affects the limbs or the spine, and the condition of the nerves that control that member, is cauterized to alleviate the numbness.
Let no one undertake this except one who is skilled in the anatomy of the limbs and of the emergence of the nerves throughout the moving body.
Chapter XLIX.
On cauterization of ulcers that become indurated.
When ulcers are old and do not respond to any of the applications of medicine, then you should cauterize upon that part which you cauterized previously, for a short period according to the quantity needed until its whiteness and color change, and its color is altered.
And then apply to that part flour mixed with peas and olive oil, and leaves of arnica, and pigeon blood or swallow blood, a portion of each, and all are blended together. Then place a cloth upon it until it is healed.
Chapter L.
On cauterization of cancer.
When cancer begins and you wish to remove it, then cauterize around it with a round cautery as it is surrounded.
And the wise say that it should be cauterized with the deepest cautery in the middle part; but I have not seen this done, for I fear it may cause an ulcer, and I have observed this several times.
And it is safer to cauterize around it in a circle as we have said, or with multiple cautery applications.
Chapter LI.
Of cauterization for hemorrhoids.
When hemorrhoids are delayed in maturation, and this is due to the excess moisture in that area or because of the patient's condition, being old and decrepit with little blood, or because of the time; if you wish to hasten the maturation of the hemorrhoids, then cauterize around them with small punctures; and afterwards apply something that will cause them to mature more quickly.
But if you want to perforate with the cautery, then use fire to heat the cautery which has this shape; and place it in the middle of the hemorrhoid until it penetrates the tissue.
And if the cautery is applied at a location that is farther away, so that the arm remains safe; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter LII.
Of cauterization for corrosive conditions.
Corrodent is a mobile corruption in the limb, which corrodes as fire consumes dry wood.
Therefore, if you see the corrodent in a place where it can tolerate cautery with fire, then heat the cautery and make small, large punctures as is appropriate in the location where the corrodent is present.
And then cauterize it thoroughly until you eradicate all the corrosion and leave no cause for it to remain.
And then dress it for three days, and apply olive oil mixed with other substances to the cauterized areas three times a day until all scars are eradicated and all corruption is eliminated.
For the treatment of that part where flesh grows.
If after three days the flesh that has grown is healthy and there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then immediately apply cauterization to what is rotten in the corrupted places.
And corrosive is the medicine for some parts with acute conditions, for that is the method instead of cauterization. But cauterization with fire works quickly and effectively.
And thus I have described the treatment with acute medicine in the division, and therefore choose that when it seems appropriate to you.
Chapter LIII.
On Cauterization of Joints and Other Similar Areas.
Often in the lower parts below the foot there is a malady called a joint, which is caused by swelling and inflammation that affects the foot; and cauterization for this condition can be done in two ways: with fire or with hot water.
But cauterization with fire is done by heating the concave cautery, resembling a ring, which is made of iron according to the size of the joint in all parts, and it should be finely polished.
And then place the heated cautery on the joint, and then rotate your hand with the cautery around the circumference of the joint until the cautery reaches the depth of the joint. And leave it for three days until it begins to form scabs. Take a plaster with wild mallows torn apart with salt and let the plaster be placed upon it for one night, for thereby its roots will be eradicated.
And then treat the area with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
And if you do not convert it, and spots appear on the surface of the body, mostly in the flesh and the bones, then when you administer cauterization with water, take a tube made of earthenware or iron or copper, and place it over the wound; then pour some hot water into the tube, and hold it tightly, and rotate it by pressing your hand all around, so that the edge of the tube is applied to the root of the wound; and as the hot water runs along the edges of the wound's root, wait for a short time until the hot water bites slightly after an hour, then remove it, for the wound with its roots will be eradicated.
And do this with each one alternately until you come over all those spots on the body.
And then treat the pus once the roots of the wounds have been eradicated with the water from the ointments that generate flesh.
Chapter LIII.
On Cauterization of Tremors.
When you find a tremor due to friction in the nerves or fever causing friction, or for some other similar reason, then it is necessary that you cauterize it with four cauteries or five on the vertebrae of the back, between each vertebra with one caustic; and at the ankles, and above the stomach, apply an olive-shaped caustic.
And when the shaking subsides, hasten the health of the patient.
Chapter .lv.
On the cautery of white spots on the body.
When on the body white spots and pus-filled blisters appear, which are formed from thick corrupted matter; therefore, at the beginning of their appearance, it should be cauterized over the entire head of the fistula with a fine cautery made of laurel wood, the end of which is extended over fire; or with long eagle's claws; or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes the cautery is applied immediately at the beginning of their expulsion according to this method; and they are not considered effective unless the excess is disrupted, after which they heal from those.
But it is necessary that this be done near evacuation with phlebotomy.
Chapter LVI.
On the cautery of accidental blood flow due to cutting an artery.
Often there is a flow of blood from an artery that has been cut due to the plague which is inside, or near the beginning of abscesses or cautery of the limb, and in similar cases, it is difficult to control that.
When such a thing happens to anyone, immediately apply your hand to the opening of the artery, and press on it with your thumb until the bleeding stops, keeping pressure so that no more blood escapes.
And then place in the fire some olive cauters, small and large, by number many, and blow upon them until they are well ignited.
And then take from them one small or one large, depending on the location and the condition where the artery is ruptured, upon that vein hastening before you have drawn breath, and hold the cauterium until it is heated and constricted that blood.
And if the blood is expelled from the orifice of the artery when you move your hand and remove the cauterium, then take another cauterium quickly from those which in the fire are prepared, and thus do not cease to do this one after another until the blood is constricted.
And be careful that you do not burn the nerve that is here, and that it does not cause other harm.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, it is not possible to constrict the blood then in those cases, especially when the artery is large, unless you use one of four methods: either by cauterization as we have mentioned, or by extraction when it has not been extracted, for when it is extracted the extremities are burned and the blood is constricted, or bind it with honey or a strong bandage, or apply medicines that have the virtue of restricting blood; and strengthen them with a cushion and proper constriction.
But he who administers proper constriction with bandages and by wrapping in cloths or by positioning burning substances and similar methods, should do so cautiously and slowly.
But if this happens to someone and there is no physician present or medicine available, then gently press with your index finger on the opening of that vein as we have said, and understand that a great constriction is needed within so that the blood stops from the main part of the wound, and do not move your finger over the artery; and sprinkle above the artery with cold water until the blood coagulates and thickens; and then decide whether cauterization or medicine is necessary.
On incision and piercing and about phlebotomy and plasters and their kinds.

And with all of those, fear and trembling accompany them, and in the worst cases of their kind, death.
And I warn you that such a thing does not fall upon you, for some cases depend on your methods, as many diseases require different approaches. Chapter 5.
On the treatment of phlegm through catharsis and the expulsion of fluids from the inner parts of the head.

And the best treatment for these conditions is as follows: that you order the patient to shave their forehead, and then make an incision in the middle of the forehead, a small one in width (and start from the temple on the left side towards the right temple; and this cut should go through the bone; extend your hand from the temples which are close to the chewing muscles), until the bone is exposed.

Chapter VI.
On the treatment of what affects the ears.
Any cause that affects the ears is one of four types: either it is a mineral stone or something similar to a stone such as iron or lead, or it is from a plant such as thorns or beans, or it is a fluid substance such as water and sour wine and things like them, or some animal.
And when a stone affects the ear or any other cause similar to those mentioned that does not adhere to or congeal in the ear, place the ear towards the sun, and if you see the stone, then administer some cause of violet oil or cumin extract at that time, and afterwards induce sneezing with certain conditions, and plug the nose upon the occurrence of sneezing. And afterwards place around the ear some pieces of cloth or wool, and extend the ear towards the upper parts, as movement several times is helpful with these causes.

Chapter VII.
On the treatment of ear obstruction.
Sometimes infants are born from their mothers' wombs with unopened ears, and this also happens in some adults, where the ear canal is obstructed by pus or flesh growing there; and this obstruction sometimes occurs at the entrance of the ear canal where it cannot be seen, and sometimes it is located in parts further out.
And the one that occurs at the entrance of the ear, which cannot be seen, is in the most difficult conditions to treat.
And in the one where you can see it, make sure to expose the ear of the patient to the sun and observe it. And if you see an obstruction that is visible, open it with a fine spatula of this form:
If the end of it where the width is small, of which one part is sharp and the other is not; and the spatula should be of two smooth sides so that it does not cause injury to the ear.
And if the obstruction is due to flesh growing in the ear or in the canal, then use fine scissors and carefully cut away all the excess flesh.
And if there is an obstruction in the ear canal, now take a thin reed and heat it over fire for a little while, and then insert that into the ear.
And so if you have detected an obstruction with what the patient finds difficult to hear due to hearing difficulty, exert pressure on it gently; and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
And then place in the ear a piece of cloth of the second size according to the width of the plug which you have wrapped in Egyptian cloth; and do this for several days until you are sure of the consolidation of the injury; or take something else and soak it in vinegar and sprinkle it with finely ground powder and use it; and if you see that the ear has become inflamed, now you should remove what you put in and replace it with another wrapped in wax mixed with olive oil until the inflammation subsides; and then continue to treat it until it is healed.
But if there is bleeding, now soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use another treatment that stops the flow of blood, which is described in the chapter on distillation.
Chapter VIII.
On the treatment of styes that occur on the eyelids.
Styes that occur on the eyelids sometimes become moist, and at other times they are dry.
Therefore, you should have those ready in small containers or in ounces, and then scatter them over the sites finely ground, and then place something finely powdered on the affected areas.
If there is pain and bleeding from a vein, then you should cauterize those that can be cauterized with a fine cautery.
And the best cauterization is near the incisions and trophic areas that return when there remains some cause for them; and when they are cauterized with fire, those causes are consumed and do not return.
Chapter IX.
Treatment for a swelling that comes to the eyelids.
Trophic areas that appear in the eyelids due to some cause that is similar to a swelling in its strength and hardness, and therefore are called swelling.
And it is an aggregation of thick humidity in the eyelid both below and above.
Therefore, the operation to be performed is that you now consider whether the swelling has arisen in the part of the eyelid that appears and affects the entire area; and the cause of this is easily addressed.
Therefore, apply something over that area along the length of the fissure, and then carefully examine it from all sides with ease until you can see clearly, and then suspend it with an ounce and bandage it.
And if the swelling is large, then you should adjust it with stitches; and care for it until it is healed.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it because the heat of it restores and renews the area.
And if the swelling is declining from the inner parts of the eyelid, you should apply a surface to the inside, thus suspending the swelling with an onion, or whatever you need for beginning, and press it all around; and if the eyelid by incision is lengthened, this does not matter in any way.
And for the incision, wash the wound with salt water; and treat the area with something that promotes healing until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
On the treatment of abscesses above the eye on the eyelid.
Abscesses are commonly formed in the layers of the eyelid above, and more frequently in children, they worsen the eyes of those affected and lead to their catarrh upon death; and they cannot bear the brightness of the sun because tears continually well up for them.

Chapter XII.

Chapter XIII.
On the treatment of an abscess that occurs in the upper eyelid.
When an abscess appears in the eye and causes contraction of the eyelid when it is closed, either to the right or left side, due to poor healing of a wound or incision or cautery, it is called leporine. This abscess can be either natural or accidental, caused by improper healing of wounds or incisions or cauteries, or similar conditions that are poorly treated.
The method of the operation is that you should cut open that consolidation and separate its edges, then place between them a piece of linen cloth, and bind it there until it heals.
And it is not advisable to use treatments for it that dry or constrict, because if you do this, the abscess will return worse than before and never heal properly.
Use treatments that soften, such as fenugreek; boil the seeds and leaves in water or myrobalan, which should be liquefied with some oil, then soak a piece of cloth in it and apply it for treatment.
The essence of your treatment is that you should pierce any part that has not healed in its proper form from the beginning.
And this operation that we have described is merely a correction of an abscess that does not return to its original form as it was before.

Therefore, when dealing with any cause of these conditions, you should consider whether it is a fresh wound or due to the natural course of the blood. Then, adjust the two sides of the wound with sutures and subsequently treat it until it heals.
And if the lips of a wound of continuity are already separated, and each one is made whole, now you must scrape each part of its visible edge until it becomes clean. And then adjust the two lips with sutures and strengthen them, and sprinkle on top of them powder of dragon's blood and thrice repeat this, and place over the powder a piece of mastic plaster or some other consolidating plaster, and leave that wound for two days or three, and then remove it and change the plaster and leave it until the pus is completely abscessed, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
The method of suturing is that you should join the wound of continuity either with needles as we have recommended for the suturing of the abdomen, or join the wound of continuity with thread as you know how to do.
Chapter XXVII.
Of the extraction of those causes that appear in the two lips in their internal parts.
Often in some men in the internal parts of their lips appear hard abscesses similar to the flesh of birds, and some of them are smaller and some larger.
Therefore, you must turn the lips and cut each of those hardnesses from all sides, and then when you abscess it, fill the place with finely shredded wool until the blood is congealed, and then wash it with vinegar and treat the area with something astringent until it is healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
On incising the flesh attached to the gums.
In cases involving the flesh attached to the gum, which the ancients called albulus, then you should suspend that part for a while or hold it with forceps and incise that part of its root, and allow the pus to flow or the blood.
And then apply to the wound dry sargings or other styptic powders or excrescences.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it again, and cauterize it, for after the cauterization it will not return.
Chapter XXIX.
On extracting teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth in both the inner and outer parts and between the gums attached, there are rough, sharp edges, tartar, and some areas are blackened and appear yellow or green, causing corruption to the gum and making the teeth decayed.
And therefore it is necessary for you to make the patient sit between your hands and hold his head in your lap, and rasp the teeth on which these rough edges appear until no such cause remains, and this will remove the blackness and yellowness and other issues so that they are cleansed.
Therefore, if this is done at the beginning of the extraction, that is good; but if not, then you should repeat the extraction on those teeth on another day and on the second and third days until it reaches the final stage as you desire.
And know that teeth require various tools of different shapes and figures according to what is prepared for your operation. And therefore there are razors of the kind used when a tooth is being extracted from inside the mouth, and others for scraping between teeth according to other forms. And these forms of razors you should prepare, which you will see shortly:
Chapter XXX.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
Take care of the tooth from its pain with all gentleness and delay in total extraction, for there is no remedy that can replace its place once it is extracted, as its substance is superior to any substitute.
But when the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, you must be certain which tooth is painful, for often the pain deceives the patient and leads them to believe it is a healthy tooth, and you extract that one but shortly after the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is removed, as we have already seen this through the experience of practitioners.
Therefore, when you must be certain which tooth is painful, and once you know well, then it is necessary that you scarify around the circumference of the tooth with a spatulum having some virtue until it dissolves the tooth from the gum completely, and then remove it with your fingers or with fine forceps little by little until it comes out.
And then use strong forceps for good leverage or a firm grip, and place the patient's head between your hands so that you hold it well without causing discomfort, and then pull the tooth straight in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause greater pain to the patient.
And so if it is not perforated, then take one of those instruments and place it all around with ease and endeavor the removal of it as was done at the beginning.
If the tooth is loose or decayed, then cover it with a piece of cloth and secure it tightly at the tip with a fine thread so that it does not break during your compression on that part while holding it.
And it is necessary that you act extremely carefully during the incision around all parts of the gum and guard against using too much strength so that you do not break or damage it, and return to the main area due to the malice that it be better than the initial pain.
And be careful not to do what some ignorant barbers do in their rashness and audacity during the extraction of teeth; for they often do not administer what we have recommended. Many times this brings about great evils to sick people, which are much harder to cure, and the roots of these problems remain entirely, or partially, or you may extract it along with some bones of the mandible as many times we have witnessed.
And after the extraction of it, the mouth should be washed with cold water or warm water mixed with salt.
And if there is bleeding due to this, as often happens, then take some quantity of sugar and sprinkle it over the wound, and if that does not suffice, cauterize it.
The shape of the forceps and subtle handles with which the tooth is held at the beginning should be of long extremity, short handle, and thick, so that they are not bent when you need to operate with them.
And so that when you insert your fingers into them, the intestines are not folded, let them be short extremities.
And let them be made of Indian iron, seamlessly made from straight extremities, and on the extremities of those let there be teeth, some of which enter into others and twist by strong constriction, and sometimes the extremities of those are in the shape of a bird's beak called ciconia, that is a heron, and they are of strong constriction.
