Chapter xxxii.
On the treatment of prominent teeth over other teeth.
When the prominent teeth do not follow their natural course and especially when the form is lagging, particularly for women and more so for those who are venerable; therefore, you should consider: if a tooth is too close to another tooth and it is impossible to extract or treat it with medicine, then you must root it out. But ensure that it is not attached to another tooth; at this point, you incise it with an instrument of this form; it resembles a boar's tusk and should be made of Indian iron with an extremely sharp edge.
And your incision should be made on the side where you will incise it over several days due to the hardness of the tooth, and ensure that it does not connect with any other teeth.
And if it is a prominent tooth that needs filing, then file it with an Indian iron file of this form:
And it should be made entirely of Indian iron and used carefully; it should have small sharp points like the file on which needles are made, with which the tooth is filed a little at a time over several days with ease, so that it does not injure any other teeth in the process. And at the end, smooth it with linen, and polish it with some razors.
And if it is a tooth of which part is broken and you run your tongue over it, then you should file down the inside until the roughness of that portion is smoothed out and evened, so that it is filled in such a way that your tongue does not get hurt and speech is not impaired.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the restoration of defective teeth with gold or silver filling.
When it happens that the front teeth are loosened or dislocated by impact or accident, and the patient cannot bite properly due to this, and you have treated them with astringent medicines but your treatment has not been effective, then in order to prevent further complications, you should restore those teeth with gold fillings and not silver ones, because silver can corrode over time, whereas gold never changes.
And the filling should be made with such subtlety and thinness according to the quantity that can fit between the teeth; the method of application is that you take the gold filling and place twice as much between two healthy teeth, then gradually extend it among one or more defective teeth until it reaches a healthy tooth on the other side. And then continue the application starting from the part where you began, and strengthen your work with skill and wisdom so that it is not defective.
And when you are applying the filling, ensure that it adheres to the roots of the teeth so that it does not come loose.
And then trim the excess filling with forceps or scissors, and carefully shape it for each location. Turn and hide these trimmed parts between a healthy tooth and a defective one so that they do not injure the tongue. And then file that part as smooth as a Roman coin.
But if it becomes loose or broken, repair that part with additional filling in such a way over time.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the treatment of two healthy teeth and of two diseased ones, as you will see these lines:
And when it happens that I return one tooth or two near the place where each should be, and they grow back as we have recounted, and they remain.
And only a good artisan and wise person can do this.
And sometimes a similar piece of cow's bone is used for a certain reason, and it is shaped like a tooth and placed in the location where the tooth is missing, and it is fixed as we have said, and it remains and serves well for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the incision of the membrane that is in the tongue and prohibits speech.
This membrane that is under the tongue sometimes is natural and one has it from birth, or it is accidental and occurs due to an injury that has already healed.
And the operation is that you open the mouth of the patient, with their head tilted to one side; and raise their tongue, and then cut that membrane along the nerve according to its width to free the tongue.
But if there is any hardness or node in that area, and this is due to the healing of an injury, then you should gently cut along the width until the membrane is divided and the node dissolved.
And be careful not to make a cut in the upper layer of the skin so that you do not sever the arteries and cause bleeding.
After washing the remnants of the incision with water or vinegar or with water and a sponge, then place linen beneath the tongue; this is the method by which the bad taste under the tongue should be washed every night so that it does not consolidate in any other way.
If bleeding occurs, place over the area vitriol triturated; but if the blood flows, immediately cauterize the site with a hot iron which is suitable for this purpose. And then treat with the other causes until it is healed.
Chapter XXXV.
On the extraction of a cyst under the tongue.
Sometimes, a swelling appears under the tongue, resembling a small abscess, which prevents the tongue from performing its natural functions; and it can grow so large that it fills the mouth.
The procedure is to open the mouth wide in front of the light and examine the swelling.
And if you see it as dark or black in color and hard, to which the swelling does not respond to touch, then do not touch it, for it is gangrenous.
And if it is inclined toward whiteness and has moisture, then you can proceed with the incision using a fine scalpel, and cut it completely; and if blood flows during your operation, immediately place over it powdered zinc sulfate, which is vitriol, to staunch the bleeding.
And then return to your work until you have treated it completely, washing it with vinegar and salt; and treat it with the other necessary care until it is healed.
Chapter xxxvi.
On the extraction of abscesses from both armpits and those in the groin and other abscesses.
When you find in the groin a gathering of glands that are somewhat assembled and appear outside, which are called armpits, when you treat them as we have said in the division and they do not heal, then consider if the abscess is hard, dark in color, and has little sensation; at this point, do not touch it with iron.
And if it is red in color and the swelling is large, then do not touch it with iron due to the flow of blood; rather, let it mature inside. Either you will then extract it or it will burst on its own.
And if it is white in color, soft, and the swelling is slight, then that should be incised.
And the operation on it is that you consider, before your operation, whether the abscess has already rested and healed after resting completely or if it has diminished in some way. Then make the patient sit facing the sunlight, with their head to your right side, and expose the mouth of the abscess, and the assistant should hold the tongue down with his fingers while you use a noble instrument of this form:
And it should be made of silver or iron, and should be as fine as a knife.
Then as you press down with that instrument and the abscess becomes visible to you, place your hand firmly over it, then make an incision and insert the instrument into the center of the abscess, and pull it outward to its full extent. Here is another tool for opening any cause of the tunics, and immediately use this instrument which has the following form:
Here is another tool for opening any cause of the tunics, and immediately use this instrument which has the following form: and it is similar to forceps, but the extremities of its are curved, thus the orifice of each of those is in line with the other, sharp, which should be made of iron.
But if this instrument is not at hand, then use the scalpel which has the following form: It is sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have made an incision, make the other following this method of incision, and then wash the wound with clean water.
But if there is bleeding, wash with water from the decoction of lemon peel or myrtle leaves and similar substances that are astringent until the flow of blood is stopped, and then treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes there appear in the neck other abscesses or those two armpits, and incise those as I have said regarding the incision of two wounds similarly.
And I once treated a woman with an abscess that had appeared in her neck, which was turning blackish in color, barely sensitive, and was already causing difficulty in breathing due to swelling. She had been prohibited from eating or drinking water, and she would have reached the point of death within another day or two if nothing had been done.
And the abscess had become so severe that it had formed two channels through which it could drain by two openings.
Thus he cut it fundamentally and placed it in one of their ounces, and pulled that out, and from there the larger portion was removed, and then he incised that where he understood the connection to the other side, and then he closed its mouth, and compressed its tongue, and then he placed the ounce in that abscess.
And then I removed a large part of it, and only a little blood came from it. Thus the woman was completely cured, and she began to drink water immediately, and then she ate food. I did not stop incising that abscess for many times over a long period, and an abscess succeeded in its place, which I incised until it was prolonged.
Thus I brought in smoke and cauterized the wound with it, thus treating the addition; and then I wandered around and did not know what God would do next regarding that.
Chapter XXXVII.
On the incision of the breast when it is abscessed.
When the milk is discharged from the breast, and it is abscessed and long, it is then called perpendicular; and if it is in the lower part thick and round, it is then called a bubo.
But when it is cured by what we have described in the division, and your treatment does not conflict with that cure, and sometimes even after cauterization, if it remains and is subtle, then you should incise the one that is aggregated and round. And if it reaches a certain length and is bloody, of dark or black color, and is not at the center, then you should attempt its incision, because there is fear of harm in such cases.
And when you see it according to the method that I have said about its whiteness and length, that you make the patient sit and press the tongue with an instrument of which shape emerges and immediately place the incision in the new area, and pull that down and dissect it with one of two instruments which are mentioned in the dissection of two membranes.
And you should not incise it beyond the natural limit without addition, for if you incise too much, you will harm their speech.
And immediately after the incision, use what we have recommended for the incision of two membranes, and care for it until it is healed.
But if the patient is terrified by the incision, use something to cauterize it without fear, and the method of the cautery in this case is nothing but sharp water. Place the head of the patient on your lap, and immediately press the tongue with the instrument we have mentioned, and then take the water which we have described in the chapter on cauterization, and apply it to that area, and place it neither thick nor thin, and with that fill the concavity of the instrument whose shape is this:
Let the extremity of what is cauterized be where the medicine is placed just as in a ladle made of brass, and place the instrument with the medicine on top, and the patient lies on their side so that saliva or mucus from the mouth does not flow out due to the internal medicine, thus preventing any cause for it to end up outside and damage that area.
And then with your hand on the medicine, apply it to the uvula for half an hour until you see that area become blackened and numb due to the biting action of the medicine.
And if you wish, take some cotton and wrap it around the extremity of the wound, then dip the cotton in the medicine, and insert both the wound and the cotton into a tube so that the cotton adheres to that tube. Do this as many times as necessary until you achieve what you want from cauterizing the grape, and afterwards let it be so that it dries up and falls off after three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, do so. Then after cauterization, clean around the wound with cotton soaked in it, and modify what is around that with the substance from the medicine, let it be washed with wet water, and treated externally with an embrocation, and proceed with cautery inside until it heals.
And this kind of treatment is safer than incision and longer in duration out of fear.
In some cases, the wound is treated with something that is gentler than cauterization and incision. It is healed through sublimation in this manner: take salve, hyssop, thyme, rue, Arabian cardamom, chamomile, and similar herbs; mix all these ingredients or some of them in a pot, and immerse them in sour wine, then boil it. The pot should be moderately heated, and at the bottom of the pot place a tray made of reeds in this way; it can be made of silver or reed.
And insert the extremity into the mouth of the pot so that the vapor reaches the wound above the tube until the wound is sufficiently covered by the steam.
And afterwards repeat this several times until it dries up, and be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the onset of abscesses, as it would be too harsh on the abscess. You should not do this; instead, you should allow the abscess to come to a head before cauterizing.
If this instrument is not at hand, take a reed and attach to it a piece of cloth at the end, so that the mouth of the patient may be protected, for the cloth of the cloth prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter XXXVIII.
On the extraction of spines and of what adheres in the gums and of other causes.
Certain objects may adhere to the gums or be caused by a fish bone or some other reason.
Then you should remove what is obvious from those objects, upon which your sight falls, and press the tongue with an instrument in front of it, so that it may be revealed to you what is in the gum.
And what does not appear and is hidden in the gum, you should make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for the cause attached to the gum will be easily expelled by vomiting; or have the patient swallow a piece of bread or lettuce leaves or a dry biscuit; or take a piece of dried sea sponge, tie it firmly with thread, and have the patient swallow that, as it passes to the place where the spine is, then it will expel it. And if you take an eel and bind it with thread soaked in honey, place it gently and remove it carefully; or take a probe and gently insert it and then carefully extract it.
And if it does not expel with the causes we have mentioned, then use an instrument made of lead in this form:
And let the pus be thick and run a small needle into it, and at its extremity make an incision which you shall insert into its mouth with facility.
And when you place the sick person upright, and be careful not to touch his bone of the throat, for so it would hurt him and those who hold him, or that the physician with his hand.
But let the patient himself insert it if he can do so skillfully, knowing the place of the cause and holding on to it, and pulling his hand with the instrument up, all this according to the quantity which is prepared within until it is properly done.
Chapter XXXIX.
On the Removal of a Bloodsucker Attached to the Throat.
When a bloodsucker is treated with what we have said in the division of treatment with medicines and not compresses, consider the hours of the day when the patient is exposed to the sun, since you have already prepared his tongue with the instrument that we have recommended.
Then if you see the bloodsucker, remove it gently with a small incision or with subtle gestures carefully.
And if it is not possible for you to remove it then, take a concave tube and insert it into the patient's throat, and subsequently place a heated iron in the concavity of the tube, and do this as many times (until the bloodsucker is dead and falls from the throat) and let the patient abstain from water for the entire day.
And then take a vessel full of fresh water and pour it into his mouth, and wash the mouth with it; and do not let any cause prevent you from doing this, and pour the water out gradually with his hand, for as soon as the bloodsucker feels the water, it will try to escape.
If it is not too much trouble with what we have recommended, then fumigate the wound with camphor or with asafetida with the instrument which we have recommended for the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it may fall out.
And this is another method of operation in fumigation of that area: take an urn in which there are burning coals and the urn is covered with a cover in the middle of which is a hole; attach the end of the instrument to that hole, and proceed with the fumigation from above. Have the patient place their mouth on the end of the tube, and close their mouth so that the fumigation does not escape until you know that the fumigation has reached the bloodletting point, for the blood will immediately come out.
And if it does not come out, then proceed with the fumigation multiple times, and prevent the patient from drinking, and give salty foods and herbs, and do not let them drink water. It is also necessary that they follow this regimen strictly.
And this is the form of the instrument with which bloodletting from the groin is performed when it is visible; and it resembles forceps as you may have seen, but it has some curvature. which is placed in the groin, and the end of it resembles the beak of a heron, which is sharpened with a file, so that when it compresses any area, it does not slip.
Chapter 40.
In which the essence of the discourse on the preparation for treating abscesses and their incision is summarized.
Abscesses are various types of swellings, of many kinds, as I recall them, each in this book.
They are diverse in their puncture and incision by two methods, of which one is according to the type of abscess itself; and by what they contain of moisture.
And the second method concerns the locations where they occur in the body, for an abscess that occurs in the head is different from one occurring in the buttocks; and an abscess in a fleshy part of the body is different from one occurring at a joint; and each has its treatment by operation.
And there are some abscesses that should not be punctured but allowed to reach maturity of the pus within them or until they come to a head.
And there are other abscesses that should be evacuated when they are small and have not reached maturity according to their full development, such as abscesses near joints, because an abscess close to a joint can extend into the surrounding area until you puncture it, which could corrupt the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing contraction of the limb; or if it is near a principal part of the body, and if you delay its puncturing while it matures, it may endanger the main member; or if it is close to the buttocks, then puncture it before it has matured; but do not puncture it when it is raw, due to incomplete digestion, except for one near the buttocks, so that it does not perforate deeply into the internal parts of the buttocks and cause a fistula or spread further without healing. But it is important that you know the right time to puncture the abscess, and this is when it has matured.
And know that it is matured when there is the cessation of pain and the subsidence of fever, and soon after the decrease in inflammation and pulse rate, and the sharpness at the head of the abscess, and other signs.
And take care that you know the puncture should be made in the place where the pus is deepest in the abscess and from which pus comes for the healing of the elevation.
And the puncture should always be made along the length of the flesh or body if the abscesses are in the parts of the arms or legs, and in places of tendons, ligaments, nerves; and finally, in all other equal places where there is no reflection; for in those that do reflect, use a puncture according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy place, it is best to wait until the internal puncture is confirmed after proper digestion of the second stage, as we have said; for if you puncture before that, it will prolong the illness of the patient and cause excessive harm, and make the lips of the abscess harden and deepen.
But some abscesses should be punctured across the length of the flesh or body due to necessity, or according to what the member requires.
And it is necessary to administer a small puncture, and in large abscesses, a larger puncture or multiple sections according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And in other abscesses where it is necessary for the incision to be hollowed out and dissected, when it is made as such, it should be done at a time when it has already become mature, just as in cases of excessive swellings and buboes.
And among those that are incised is one having three angles; and of this kind is the one which is incised as in the figure of a myrtle leaf, like the abscesses of the fingers.
And among those to which round incision and lunar incision are administered, and similar ones to these.
But in the case where none of these applies, such as with superficial lion abscesses, you should puncture it with a superficial puncture or simply make it flat.
And when the abscess is large and has already collected pus and sediment, you should puncture it but do not hesitate to extract all the pus at that time; having extracted one part from it, on another day incise the abscess inside, and then extract a larger portion, and continue doing this several times according to its gradation until it is fully drained.
And especially if the patient is a young man, or pregnant woman, or small child, or elderly in debility, for in such cases the animal spirit is quickly resolved with the evacuation of the pus, and the patient easily recovers, and you should observe them.
Therefore, guard this chapter with great caution, and after your puncture in which you pierce these abscesses, make sure to cleanse the plaques and then consider whether the plaque of the abscess is small or a flat incision; at that time administer some of them or of antiseptic cloth. And if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the puncture are many, then you should place wax in each tract until they heal together.
And if in the abscess you have divided a part of the cord or have concave it around the circuit, then you should fill it with old cloth or thread made from linen, as when you have treated pustules or roots, without moisture; and strengthen that on the third day; and thereafter proceed to treat it according to what is needed from the remedies until it is healed.
If blood flows during the time of your operation, then prepare water and vinegar in which to dip a cloth, and place that over the spot where the blood is flowing for several times.
If the blood continues to flow, then you should administer treatment and healing, using powders which I have recommended in various places of this our book and division.
If it is in the time of winter, and the location of the abscess is near many nerves, then you should infuse powders in vinegar and burnt oil, and place those on the affected areas.
And if it is in summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you should place powders soaked in water and oil or in vinegar and oil, and keep this moist until the third day, as we have said. Then you should open the abscess, clean it, and administer to its healing whatever is necessary until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses generally, but according to the method of distinction I have already said about each abscess in what manner is the way of its cure separately by chapters.
Chapter XLIV.
On the sectioning of abscesses that occur in the neck.
In the neck of the head, small abscesses may occur, along with various types of nodes that contain membranes; and these types vary in many ways.
And they are soft, and those that contain moisture similar to jelly. Of those things which contain humidity similar to dust, and resembling them; and of those things which are stony, hard.
And in the section of all things, and in the extraction of those [things], there is no fear, as the extraction of those does not oppose nature.
And the operation in the section of those [things] is that you express them with an instrument whose form is appropriate for this, which is called an entromes, until you know what it contains.
For if what it contains is humidity, then you should divide that with a section along its length according to a superficial section in this form:
And begin the section from the line of .B. to the line of .G. When the humidity is evacuated, then you should excise that skin which contains the humidity, and do not leave any cause for harm, as closely observed wounds return if any cause remains.
And then immerse the wound in Egyptian ointment if it is available, and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with this and leave it for another day, as this will dissolve all that remains of the membrane.
And then repeat the treatment on that wound with Egyptian ointment a second and third time if necessary, until you are confident that there is no remaining cause of that membrane; now treat the wound with the ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains hard lumps, then you should incise it according to this form: with a section having the shape of a cross, and extract once between two lips of the section and scrape it thoroughly from all sides, and exert your skill in extracting the membrane that contains it.
If a wound from an arrow causes trouble, then at that time do what I have recounted first.
And similarly, treat the abscess that has formed through section and treatment as we have said to you; and section on an abscess that has formed is easier, because it contains little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes there are some of these abscesses that occur in the head in certain people who do not have much moisture throughout their body; the cause is that I once incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found it to be like a hard, rough, white stone, which could not be broken, and if anyone were to hit it, they would hurt themselves.
However, in other abscesses that occur in the head, this is not the case with this chapter; also, regarding abscesses that appear on children's heads and behind their ears, the section for all of these should be a superficial incision.
And pause the puncturing of those in the lower part so that the contents of the abscess can drain into the lower parts.
And treat those accordingly with what is appropriate for that type of treatment.
Chapter XLII.
On the incision of scrofulas that occur in the neck.
These abscesses often occur in the neck, armpits, and groin; and sometimes they appear in other parts of the body.
And those that occur in the neck often develop between the muscles, and every scrofula among them is within its own proper layer, just as with knots and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
The species of these scrofulas are spots, for only some of them are stony, and of those only the ones containing moisture, and of those only the malignant ones which do not obey cure.
Then in that one which you find to be well disposed upon examination, the color of which is clearly manifest and matches the color of the skin, and it is soft throughout, and it is not attached to the nerves of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, and it is not hard; proceed to incise that at a superficial dryness above the part and below the body according to line .G. between the line .B., and peel that off entirely, and extend from the edges of the skin by one inch or two inches if you need those, as we have said concerning the abscesses of the head, and extract it little by little.
And it is more cautious that the vein or nerve not be incised.
And let the spatula not be sharp, so that it does not cause you to cut unnecessarily with your hand, or disturb the patient, and do not incise what is not necessary to incise; for if you were to cut a vein or artery during your operation, immediately apply a green vitriol plaster or other powders which promote blood flow, and staunch the bleeding, and leave it there until the acute stage of the abscess subsides and the inflammation becomes mollified and begins to pus. When the blood is constricted.
Then return to your operation once you have completed it.
And immediately inquire at the time of the operation if there is any cause for the other small scrofulas, then incise those and clean them.
And if it is in the root of the scrofula a large vein, avoid incising that scrofula at its root. And be careful to bind that one with a firm double bandage and let it fall by itself without harm.
And then fill the wound with old lint which you have already soaked in Egyptian ointment, and then treat that area.
And if you incise that scrofula completely, then be sure to approximate the two lips of the wound and sew them together immediately so that there is no room for any superfluity.
But if you see that there is room for superfluous fluid due to the size of the scrofula, then be sure to incise its root and evacuate it and administer sutures as described.
And for the scrofulas that contain moisture, make a superficial puncture where it appears to you the place of maturation is, and pause before making another puncture deep into the base of the mass, as we have said.
And then administer punctures, cover them with ointment and similar applications, so that it aids in the corruption of what remains to be corrupted. And when you see the wound already cleansed, then treat it with an ointment of generative flesh so that it may be healed.

On the division of the pus duct of an accidental abscess in the groin.

Chapter XLIV.
On the incision for an abscess that occurs in the windpipe outside, which is called a hernia of the windpipe.
This swelling, called the hernia of the groin, is a large swelling according to the colour of the body, and it occurs in women.
And it happens in two ways, for it is either natural or accidental.
And in the natural case, there is no need for treatment.
And the accidental case happens in two ways; one of which is similar to fat nodes, and the other way is similar to a swelling caused by an artery knot, and in that case there is reason for concern. And it is not appropriate to make an incision with a knife except in the case where it is small. And you should examine that and investigate it carefully with the finger, and if you find it similar to a fat node and not attached to any veins, then you incise it as you would with nodes and extract what is inside like that of hair and bursiclam; otherwise, you must thoroughly explore its nature, and subsequently treat the area with whatever is necessary for healing until it is healed.
Chapter XLV.
On the Section of the Kinds of Nodes.
There are various kinds of nodes, and I have already mentioned all their kinds in the division.
And it is necessary for me to describe as well the difference between a node and an abscess so as to cause no doubt.
Therefore, I say that in an abscess there is heat and fever and severe pain until the fermentation of the excess matter subsides; and it completes putrefaction at which point the fever, pain, and heat cease.
This may not be heat nor fever nor pain, and it continues as if there were a lion's paw in that place.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a boil and it is made thus whether larger or smaller, and they are of two kinds, either dry containing heat, or greasy and containing moisture, and the colors of the moisture are motley, as I say in the division.
Therefore, when you come to the treatment of this condition, express it and inquire about its beginning at the moment which is called intromission, as its form will come in the chapter which is near this one.
And this is the method of inquiry for abscesses and all nodes, which is that you take this moment and insert it into the place most humid which you find in the abscess, and you will revolve it little by little until you know that the moment has already penetrated the tissue.
And according to the measure of the size of the abscess, you proceed accordingly, then extract the moment and observe what has come out in its wake.
And if the flowing moisture, of whatever color it may be, is found, then you incise it with a superficial cut as I have said regarding other abscesses.
And if from the wake of the moment you do not find moisture, then you know that it is greasy.
And make an incision over it in the shape of a cross according to this form: as I have taught you, and suspend it with threads, and scrape the tissue from all sides with ease, and preserve the skin or sac, if possible, over that area, and extract the pus from the node.
If the maxilla is broken during the operation, it is not possible to extract it whole, and you must break it up into pieces so that you can remove it bit by bit until nothing remains.
If any part of it breaks off for whatever reason, whether little or much, the remainder will return to its place according to the maxilla.
And if you see that any small part remains broken off, then apply a poultice of some corrosive powders and clean the wound, and place over it an abscess cauterizing agent, and treat it with another method until it is healed.
And if the remaining piece is large, then you should suture both sides of it and treat it with something that promotes healing.
If a pulsating or non-pulsating vein is encountered, then tie it off and apply the dressing with vitriol three times so that the wound heals and the blood clot stabilizes; and afterward return to separate what is broken from the remainder.

Chapter XLVII.
On the treatment of men's breasts that are similar to women's.
Sometimes the breasts of some men swell due to an excess during sleep when they begin to mature, and subsequently their breasts swell and resemble those of women, and we call them Roman apostems or abscesses.
And for that which you wish to excise, cut it in this lunar section from the line of .B. to the line of .G., then scrape away all the roughness, then fill the wound with flesh-generating medicine, then stitch together the two lips of the wound, and care for it until it is healed.
If the mammary gland inclines towards the sides and is softened by its size just as it does in women, then cut it into two parts with its pus inside, each part in sections resembling a lunar shape, of which each should be contained within the other at their ends, such that the larger line contains the smaller one according to this form from the line of .B. and the line of .G. Then scrape away the tissue between the two lips of the wound, remove the fat, and administer what we have mentioned about suturing and powders as necessary until it is healed.
And if you do not complete the excision of what needs to be cut due to the patient's discomfort and bleeding, then fill the wound with cotton soaked in a corrosive liquid and leave it until this corrodes what remains of the fat, and then care for it until it is healed.

Chapter XLIX.
On the incision of abscesses that occur due to artery or vein.
When an artery is ruptured and the part where it was located becomes inflamed, an abscess may occur. The signs by which one can recognize whether inflammation and abscess are due to an artery or a vein: for when the abscess is due to an artery, it tends to be elongated near the front of the body; and if you press on the abscess with your fingers, you will hear a sound like a whistle.
And when it is due to a vein, it is round on the surface of the body.
And the incision of this abscess is dangerous, especially when it occurs in the armpits, in the angles, in the neck, and in many parts of the body; it is serious, therefore care should be taken to avoid its surgical treatment, and if it is in the extremities or on the head, it should be avoided even more.
But in the case where it is due to inflammation of the artery opening, incise along the length of the part; and then carefully cut with hooks near the artery, and remove the tunics until it is exposed.
Upon that needle I place and make it penetrate into the other side, and tie the artery with fine thread in two places, which procedure I explain in extracting two arteries, which are found in the temple.
And then apply the place between two bandages with a poultice so that the blood comes out completely while it is there, and let the abscess be dissolved.
And then administer treatment in which pus is generated so that the bandages can fall off; and subsequently treat it with appropriate remedies until it is healed.
And if it is an abscess due to the section of a vein, now you should have in your hand what is possible regarding the abscess and the condition, and then insert the needle below the place where you had below your hand, and make it penetrate; and tie it with double thread until you extract that from the other side, and subsequently ligature the abscess with a strong bandage as I have told you in the bandaging of vine leaves on both sides of the thread's extremities.
But if you fear that the thread will come out, then insert the thread with that needle below all the abscess where the first incision was made, and tie the threads in four places.
And then dry the abscess in its proper place so that when what is inside comes out, you incise the excess of the tissue, and leave what from it is adhered.
And then apply powder that you have submerged in wine and oil, and subsequently administer the treatment with dressings and remedies until it is healed.
Chapter on the abscess which occurs due to nerve strain.
Just as an abscess occurs in arteries and veins, so too does an abscess occur in nerves when they are struck or due to excessive labor, and similarly.
And it happens mostly in the areas of the joints or heel, or in any place where the joints are often troubled.
It is a severe abscess and its color resembles the surrounding tissue. It is one of the worse conditions when it occurs with vehemence, and the patient appears in a state similar to stupor.
It is not an abscess that aggregates at the front of the body, and because of this it is less painful. It affects the entire area equally and does not concentrate in front or back.
Therefore, when it occurs in the joints, it should not be bandaged with iron, for it will likely lead to contraction.
But when it occurs in the head or on the forehead, apply treatment with spatum (a type of poultice). But if it is a small abscess, then treat it with forceps and incise it carefully.
But if it is large, then suspend it with ligatures, and excise it properly; afterwards, close the wound with sutures, and care for it until it heals.

On the treatment of an umbilical hernia.
An umbilical hernia is caused by various reasons; either by fissures in the abdominal wall above the abdomen, and through these openings the membrane or bowel protrudes as in other ruptures; or by a wound from which blood flows from a vein or artery depending on its location, or by trapped air.
If it is due to a fissure in the abdominal wall and the protrusion of the membrane, then the color of the swelling is similar to the color of the skin; it is soft without pain, and afterward appears in various situations. And if it is due to the protrusion of the bowel, then its appearance differs greatly from what we have described, and when you press on it with your fingers, it retreats, and afterward returns; and it is very sensitive to sound, and swells after bathing and hard work.
May it be soft because of moisture, so that when you press upon it with your hand, it is not hidden, nor is it constricted, nor is it hardened.
And if it is due to pus, then at this point these signs appear, the abscess turning to a declining blackness.
But if it is due to excess flesh, then the abscess is severe, large and hard, and is seated in proportion to a quantity.
But if it is due to airiness, then the touch of it is soft.
And the operation in it is that you consider now whether the prominence of the navel is due to pus from an artery, or a vein, or airiness; now it behooves you to be cautious about treating it, for there is fear and dread as I have taught you in the chapter wherein I have stated the abscesses from an artery and a vein.
And if the prominence of the navel is due to the bowels or the membrane, then it behooves you to command the patient to lie on their back and extend themselves; then feel around the entire circumference of the navel, and then command them to stand between your hands on their back; then surround with a spatula at the edge of the navel where you have felt with your hand; then cut half of the abscess above one inch greatly; then tie around the edge with strong thread or cord in an appropriate manner.
And then cut the center of the abscess extended over the ligature and insert your finger into that place to check for the bowels; and if you find them or guard them with the ligature, then you extract promptly and push the bowels to the sides within the abdomen.
And if you have found the cloth, then take one ounce of it; and incise the superfluities from it.
But if it is contrary to your artery or vein, then encircle that spot, and return to your operation; take two needles and place them in those two strong threads and insert the two needles in the circuit which you have made around the abscess in a crisscross manner, which you should make penetrate; and then compress the abscess into four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and let the place inside which the extended flesh may ooze and fall off by itself; or incise it when it has become softened and will ooze out, and then treat it with water from the herbs until it is healed.
And if the swelling of the navel is made of flesh or because of humidity, then you should conquer the abscess as we have said and extract the blood or moisture that you find there, and then treat it with what is appropriate to heal and consolidate the area.
Chapter LIII.
On the Treatment of Wounds.
We have already said in the division about the types of wounds and in what manner they are treated with medicines, and that it is necessary for the treatment to be done carefully so that it does not become ulcerated; we have also discussed wounds born out of carelessness during their treatment.
And the ancients said that in some cases wounds can be treated in a location where it is possible to eradicate them completely, such as wounds in the nipples or in the armpits and similar parts of the body allocated for removal, especially when they are beginning small.
When it is old and hard, do not attempt to treat it yourself, for I cannot cure such a thing nor have I seen anyone else succeed in doing so.
And the operation should be performed when it is prepared as we have said, that you proceed and allow the patient to purge from black bile several times, and then bleed them if a clear abundance of pus is found.
And then prepare the patient for the operation in which it will be performed, and then place hooks on the wound as is necessary; and afterward wash all around with a good linen cloth to clean inside so that there remains no cause for infection. And let the blood flow freely, and do not press it too roughly, and squeeze out the areas, and remove the thick blood with your hand or with an instrument that is available to you among the tools.
But if it is contrary to your ability to stop thick blood clotting from a vein or artery, then cauterize that area until the flow of blood stops, and afterward treat it with other methods until it is healed.
Chapter .liv.
On the treatment of hydrops (edema or fluid accumulation).
We have already recommended, in the division, the types of hydrops related to water, and the signs of each type, along with their treatment using medicines.
And the type that is treated with iron is only the single type of pleurisy, and not approached with iron for the types of tympanites or hydrothorax, for this is fatal.
And when you have already treated this kind of tympanic hydrocephalus with medicines and do not confirm your treatment, now consider whether the patient has reached a state of debility or suffers from another illness along with that hydrocephalus, just as if it were in the chest or belly area and similar to those cases. Now guard yourself that you do not treat them with iron, for there is fear concerning this.
And if you see the patient with excessive vigor, and in them there is no other illness but hydrocephalus alone, and they are neither a child nor elderly, then the method of the operation for them is that you lift the patient between your arms, and the assistant approaches to press the abdomen of the patient with their hand, pushing the water out from below the navel, and then take spatum spinos in this form:
It should be made of two sharp parts long, with a pointed end like a lancet; however, it should gradually become shorter in length, so that during the operation you do not pierce into the bowel and injure it.
And then consider whether the origin of the hydrocephalus is from the bowels; if so, you should distance the incision from the navel by a length of three fingers below it, directly over the navel.
But if the origin of the hydrocephalus is from a kidney illness, then your incision should be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if the origin of it is from the liver, then your incision should be on the right side.
And it is not advisable for your incision to be on the side on which the patient lies, so that the fluids do not flow to that place and cause harm.
With an instrument, perforate entirely the wound, and then insert it into that section, and raise your hand with a spatula between the wound and the tissue, and you will scrape off that part; and if there is a quantity of one layer or around about.
And then I perforate the tissue so that the spatula reaches the empty space, which is where the water is. And then extract that part and insert an instrument of this shape: and it is similar to a thin silver cannula; it is made of silver or ivory or lion's bone, at the lower part of which there is a small hole, and on its sides are three holes: two from one side and one from the other side; and the tip of it is cut in the manner of a pen, as you can see.
And at the uppermost part of it is a ring, because when the instrument reaches the water, then immediately you should stop above the instrument, thus evacuating only a small amount of water at once, for if you evacuate as much as does not fit in one go, the patient may die from resolution of their vital spirit, or it will lead to imminent death.
But evacuate from that according to the quantity of the patient's strength and according to what is indicated by the condition of the patient, the strength of their pulse, and their overall well-being.
And then extract the instrument, and retain the water that is because it is immediately retained by the wound which holds the hole that is on the tissue of which you are instructed to perforate according to that method.
And prepare it again with the instrument on another day if the patient tolerates that, and extract from the water a small quantity, and do this with some days in between out of fear, and guard yourself against error so that you do not run out of the water before its time.
But if you fear for the patient, and give a large quantity of water, then bury him in fine sand, and sprinkle moisture on him in baths, and let him wait long hours in the sun; and treat him with drying medicines until he is healed.
And cauterize the stomach and the intestines after the extraction of the water, as I have recounted in what preceded.
Chapter 55.
On the treatment of children who are born with unopened passages for urine.

And then insert a fine lead needle into the passage and secure it, and leave it in for three days or four.

And if you do not insert the lead, it is possible that the urine which passes over that area will not allow this opening.

Let those in which the wound is not in its place be treated as mentioned, the cause of which is that he who is born and the wound is behind the tip of the penis so that he cannot urinate to the sides; and it is not for generation, so that the semen cannot reach the major second rectitude, and this is a laghid disease.

And be careful during your operation to stop bleeding, as this often happens; and pause to address the bleeding before you proceed with the incision, and treat the wound until it is healed.

because such cautery is beneficial in two situations simultaneously: for bleeding and for the corruption of wounds.

Chapter 60.

Chapter LXI.
On the Extraction of Stones from Women.
Stones in women are rarely formed.

The second reason is that you do not find many women who are honest enough to confess to their condition, whether they are unmarried or married.
The third reason is that you do not find many women who are knowledgeable about this art, especially the operation itself.
The fourth reason is that the location for the incision to remove a stone in women is far from the location of the stone, which requires a large incision, and there is fear associated with it.
However, if necessity compels you to do this, then it is necessary for you to find an experienced and honest woman surgeon, but few are found.
However, if you do not have access to such a person, then seek a skilled and careful man surgeon, or present a midwife who is knowledgeable about women's conditions, or a woman who has some part of the knowledge in this art.

And similarly in treating this as you would treat other wounds, when you incise the tissues, clean that area and restore the wound before it is consolidated; for the mesentery is contracted and does not adjust to the prominence of rupture.
And cautery is best in this place, for it is closer to health.
Chapter LXVIII.
On the softening of the cord of the testicles.
Often the cord of the testicles is softened in some men because the tissues that are within it are softened, and this requires careful attention.
Then it is necessary for the one who is asked to treat this condition to place the patient in a position according to his need, and incise the entire cord which is softened over the natural cord; and then adjust two edges with suturing.
And if you wish, cut away the excess of the softened cord at three or four places and secure it with firm sutures.
And then incise between the sutures; and then treat as you would other wounds until it is healed and the tissues have knitted.
Chapter LXIX.
On Castration.
Know that castration in our law is excommunicated, but for the cause it is read to me which makes me remember it in this book; and the remembrance of it is not necessary except for two ways, of which one is so that when someone is asked about it by a physician who seeks treatment, he knows the cure for it when it happens; and the other way is that we have sometimes need of castration of other beasts for our use through them, such as horses, oxen, and pigs, and similar to these animals.
And thus I say that castration is performed in two ways: either by excision or by division and cutting.
But in the one that is performed by excision, the method of the operation is that we should bathe the animal in warm water until the testicles become softened and relaxed and hang down. And then take those with your hand until they are released and do not appear below the touch.
But in castration performed by division and cutting, make sure you hold the animal and press the scrotum of its testicles with your left hand.
And then lift the suspensory ligament, and make an incision on each side with a cut so that two sides are processed.
And then make an incision on those parts after you have peeled each one. And do not leave any cause for infection on those parts, but rather make the incision subtle so that it is above the veins.
And this method of castration is better, which is done by inversion, because inversion leaves little cause for the animal to desire sex.
And properly treat the illness until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
On the treatment of hermaphrodism.
Hermaphrodozia in men is according to two methods: one is when there appears in the space between the testicles, where hair of a female nature grows.
And sometimes in that case urine is discharged from the place between the testicles.
In women, there is one type: it is that the female genitalia above the pubis appears as two testicles of a man slightly below and forward as the scrotum of a man with two testicles.
And the treatment is of three types: two for men and one for women. It is that you should cut away those tough tissues until the excess is removed, and then treat them as you would other illnesses.
And in the second type for men, from whom urine is discharged from the place between the testicles, there is no treatment or surgery to be performed.
Chapter 21.
On the treatment of hanging and protruding flesh due to causes related to women.
This dark spot is usually light in color and is adjusted according to natural causes within which it is situated and made legible with regard; and some are magnified in some women so much that it spreads just as in men and reaches up to the act of intercourse.
Then you should remove the excess of the spot with your hand or with unguent and incisions.
And do not proceed with incisions, especially near the root of the hair, so that there is no flow of blood.
And immediately treat that with the care used for wounds so that it may be healed.
Hidden flesh is the flesh that grows in the fold beneath the mother's tail where it fills that area, and it is usually situated at the parts around like a tail (and for this reason some of the earliest called this "tail disease"); then you should incise it just as we do with spots, and treat it so that it may be healed.
Chapter LXXII.
On the Treatment of Alopecia.
Alopecia is when the woman's hymen is not perforated, or the opening is small. And it can be either natural or accidental.
It is accidental due to a previous illness, and that is caused either by flesh poorly adjusted or by subtle or thick phlegm.
And it occurs either near the root of the tail, or on its sides, or at the upper part of it, or at the lower part, and you should prohibit intercourse and conception and childbirth, and usually menstruation is also prohibited.
Of that knowledge is made by observation of the menstruation, if the obstruction is apparent, proceed; but if it is not apparent, then at that time do around it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is due to fine scabs and it can be reached with two forceps, then grasp it and remove it.
And place sponges over the two forceps, and then place your fingers or probes near both sides.
And the woman should lie on her back, her buttocks raised, and then spread the two forceps forcefully so that the fine scab is broken and the obstruction is opened.
And then take some lint and soak it in oil and place that over the area, and have the patient return daily so that the cut does not heal too quickly.
But if the scab is thick and dense, then you should cut it with a scalpel or a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is due to flesh, then lift it up slightly and cut it.
And have with you the medicines that stop blood flow without causing pain, such as hystiatia, dragon's blood, and ensus, mixed with egg white.
And then administer a lead tube that is wide so that the wound does not heal too quickly.
And keep that in place for several days and administer dry lint.
And let it be treated along with the other treatment for consolidation until it is healed.
And in some cases accidents occur during the major separation of other tissues, and at such times it is necessary to incise according to this method. But if this is due to chronic abscesses, do not make any opposition to the abscess that is in the major part in any way.
Chapter LXXIII.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids and similar conditions that occur in the genital area of women.
Hemorrhoids are appreciated as the openings of small veins from which blood flows continuously; and when these hemorrhoids become old, they form into nodes.
And now I have mentioned in the division the species of these and the signs of them, and to say which of them receive treatment and which do not receive treatment.
Therefore, I say that hemorrhoids and nodes when they are in the vicinity of the major part and do not appear to the senses, at such times there should be no treatment applied to them.
But those that are at the opening of the major part and which can be felt, these are the ones that are treated.
And you must place the woman in a comfortable position and then extend those nodes with forceps or with a rough cloth, and incise them with scissors.
And then powder should be applied to them to promote the flow of blood from one of the powders used for incisions without chewing, such as acacia and similar substances.
Then warm his feet for an hour, and then immerse them in cold water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then have him sit in a decoction of sumac, orange peels, walnuts, and similar substances.
Then take flour of barley and mix it with honey and vinegar to make a plaster, which you should apply to his back.
And if the blood is clotting, that is good; but if not, apply cupping glasses to the back and make incisions at the breast.
Then take wool and soak it in the juice of cilantro or anise or fennel mixed with sour wine, and apply it to the affected area; then treat the area with ointments until it is healed.
And treat the area until it is healed.

Chapter LXXVI.
On the Extraction of a Dead Fetus.
When you treat a dead fetus as we have said in the division of treatment and it does not come out on its own, and you are forced to use surgical intervention, then it is necessary for you to consider whether the woman is of sound constitution and is not subject to any of the accidents that lead to fear of loss.
Now it is necessary for the woman to be placed on her back on the bed with her buttocks raised and her head lowered.
And the attendant should hold her in both parts or tie her to the bed so that her body does not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you should moisten the mouth of the fetus with oil, honey, and mucus made from alum, fenugreek, and linseed.
And then the midwife should anoint her hand with those oils and mucus, and then gently insert her hand into the fetus, and then search with her hand for the position where the hooks can be placed in the fetus.
And appropriate places for this are as follows: then you should consider if the fetus is positioned to descend by its head, and now you place the hooks in its hands, or in the neck, or in the mouth, or in the palate, or under the chin, or in the clavicle, or in proper places on the ribs, or under the armpits.
If the swelling is on the legs, then apply it to the navel, or to the middle of the back.
And he should hold the bandage in his right hand, and place the curve of it at the point where his left hand's fingers are positioned, and insert the hand with ease so that the bandage is placed in one of those places we have mentioned, until it reaches the swollen area.
And he should place and fix it in such a way that another bandage, or a third if necessary, is applied to provide equal traction, and not simply extension according to its straightness alone.
And it should be done gently in all parts, so that the swelling is alleviated just as it is done around the area to be treated.
And it is necessary in this that the extension be softened; and if any part of it is restrained, then the midwife should anoint some of the said substances with oil and insert them carefully and rotate around what is restrained.
Therefore, if a part of the fetus is protruding, then the bandages should be moved to a place that is easier; and do this until all of the fetus is delivered.
But if one hand is swollen in front of the other, and it is not possible to reduce it by compression, then cloth should be wrapped around it; and subsequently extract it until all of it is delivered, and cut it either at the wrist or at the elbow.
And do the same for the other hand and both legs.
If the swelling of the fetus is large, or if it affects the skull during delivery, or if it occurs in the head of the tightly wrapped umbilical cord, then one must intervene between the spined spatulas and cut open the head to remove the fluid, or use an instrument called “alundat,” the form of which will be among the forms of other instruments described near this chapter.
And similarly if the fetus has a large head by nature: then it must be intervened with some kind of restraint, or taken out using an instrument called “alundat” as we have said; and afterwards extract the bones using forceps.
And if the head is delivered and is constricted behind the clavicle, then it must be cut so that the fluid in the membranes can be released.
But if it is not complicated and does not obstruct delivery, then you must cut the head as soon as possible according to what seems appropriate to you.

And if the abscess is on the side, then at this time, if possible, it should be lanced: and that which we have said about the living fetus should be administered.
And if this is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces and extracted.

And when you have completed all of your operation, then apply that which we have said about the treatment of abscesses that occur on the mother.
And if bleeding occurs, then bathe in astringent waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have seen a woman in whose womb was a dead fetus, and right above that focus another pregnancy occurred, and the other focus was a dead fetus. An abscess occurred near the navel for a long time; it became swollen and internally inflamed, and began to discharge pus.

Of the treatment of the unopened anus of a child.
Many children are born with their anuses not open, which is small, closed up. Then it is necessary for the midwife to carefully open that small closure with her finger; and if not, she should use a sharp probe to do so, taking care not to touch the membrane.

Of the treatment of fistulas that occur nearby.
Fistulas that occur in the lower part near the anus are usually small and thick, which appear close to the anus or on one side.
And it is either one fistula or multiple.
And as that small area becomes old and drains watery white pus or subtle purulence, sometimes these fistulas penetrate deeper.
And these penetrating ones are known by what is expressed from them through excretion and gas formation; and sometimes there is an obstruction in excretion, and it easily indicates the nature of these issues.
Of those fistulas some extend into the space penetrating into the bladder, and into the shaft of the penis; and of those penetrating into the joint of the thigh and into the root of the genitals.
And in which the penetrating and non-penetrating fistula is known, you should insert your finger into the anus, and insert a fine probe of horn or iron into the fistula when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in that, now insert a fine probe of lead or horsehair into it until you feel the probe or hair with your finger.
And if you do not feel any of this nor proceed with the treatment for any cause of evacuation such as worms or flatulence, as we have said, then you know that it is non-penetrating, and now apply yourself to its cure.
And when the fistula is penetrating into the bladder or into the shaft of the penis, then the significance of it is that urine comes from there, and the prohibition is such that the site must be healed by medical treatment.
But if it is penetrating into the joint of the thigh or into the bone and there is pain in the circumference, then the sign of it is that the probe passes through here if there is no tortuosity within the bone, and there is pain in the circumference of the affected area; and you will have an abscess permanently; and treatment with medication does not work there, nor is it healed by application.
And in all those penetrating fistulas there is no complete cure, and the treatment for these is the labor and effort of skilled physicians upon which it depends.
In that which is not penetrating and not ancient, healing by iron is hoped for as I will tell you, and this is what I and my predecessors have done.

Of the treatment of plagues.
I have already said in the section on the treatment of plagues what was appropriate for the book, and I will now describe the treatment of those with medicine according to the completion.
Therefore, I say that plagues are diverse according to what causes the plagues and according to the location where the plague occurs.
And the causes for which plagues occur are such as stone retention, or cutting by a sword or knife, or striking with a spear, or with a stick, or with an arrow, or similar to those from various causes.
Here are the plagues that are in the parts of the body such as the plague which affects the head, the neck, the feet, the belly, the groin, and similar ones in the limbs.
And I also recommend the treatment of certain plagues, so that you pause that reasoning and rule regarding other plagues.
And I will begin with the simple plagues of the head, for the recollection of the compound ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say that when you encounter a simple plague in the head and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is due to a blow from a stone or similar object, and if it has only bruised the bone but caused a large swelling and you fear that there may be an abscess underneath, then immediately examine and perform phlebotomy on the cephalic vein without delay; do not postpone this unless there is some prohibiting cause for phlebotomy, especially if little blood comes out of the wound.
And let the phlebotomy be performed on the opposite side of the affected part; and protect the patient from overeating and from heavy foods.
But if some reason prevents you from performing phlebotomy, then reduce the patient's food intake and do not allow meat or heavy foods.
And pause treatment on the plague if an abscess forms underneath; then apply only rose oil or in a liquid that has been stypticated.
But if you are certain of an abscess underneath, then pause treatment on the plague if it is still fresh and the blood and air have not changed the powder that I recommend to apply shortly.
And if the air has already changed that, pause at certain intervals until it is healed.
And if the wound is large or due to a fracture and the lips of it cannot be approximated, then approximate those with stitches as I will describe regarding the stitching of them.
And if the wound is already exposed to the bone and flesh, and there is no need to retain it except for a small suspensorium, then incise it and treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh in place of stitches, and command the patient to be nourished on foods that are nutritious: such as meat, eggs, and similar items until he recovers.
And if you find in the wound putrefaction and it does not adhere to the bone, then incise all that is purulent and remove it, and subsequently treat it.
And if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which bleeding could occur, and it cannot be stopped by medicines, then you should inquire about the condition of the artery. If you find that it has not been cut, then you should cut it with a scalpel or ligate it; and if necessity compels you to do something different from what we have said, then you should cauterize until the bleeding is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, the cause of them is minor. Therefore, you can powder those with this powder, as it will consolidate them before the air changes them.
And the method of the powder is as follows: take oil of sesame one part, dragon's blood two parts, and burnt frankincense three parts; mix them thoroughly and sift. Apply this to fill the wound, and bind it tightly so that it adheres well to the wound, and let it coagulate with the blood.
And bind tightly above the poultice that which is below it and leave it bound until such time as it is not inflamed, for medicine does not reach it while it remains unsanated.

And the needles should be of medium fineness between coarseness and subtlety, for fine needles penetrate the flesh too quickly; and thick ones are difficult to insert in the tendon, and therefore it is fitting that they be of medium fineness and thickness.
And it is not fitting for the needle to be placed at both ends of the tendon too close together, so that the flesh is not penetrated quickly and the wound does not separate before its consolidation.
And do not extend the stitches too far apart so that the wound is not hindered from consolidating.
And then cut the ends of the needles so that they do not bother the patient while sleeping; and place over the stitches pads expanded from all sides, which should cover the ends of the needles; and leave them in place until you know that the wound has already consolidated.
And this type of stitching with needles is most convenient for small wounds, as you often need only one or two needles or around that number.
But the second type of stitching is common: it is that you manage in one go the four extremities, of the tendon and two ends of the skin, with a needle which is properly wrapped in subtlety and thickness.
When the needle has penetrated these four extremities, withdraw the needle from that middle part from which you began, so that the thread exits at the most prominent part of the wound, so that the suture is a cloth-like stitch in which the threads are tightly drawn.
And pause between each suture by the width of the smallest finger.

On the treatment of abscesses and fistulas.
Know that every wound and swelling when it suppurates and becomes old, and forms an ulcer, and is not consolidated and continues to discharge for a long time without being excised, whether in any part of the body, is universally called a fistula, and we call it that kind of abscess.
And truly a fistula is a felted node, hard and white, at which there is no pain but which itches like the itch of bird's mites; and for this reason it is called that kind of rash, that is, an itching affliction.
And it is in some people a moist condition, which discharges constantly; and the moisture is easily excised within a few hours. And at times this moisture is thick, and it is little, and it is coarse, and it is fine.
I have shown all those causes, and the signs of them, and the cure for each with medicines in the division.
And it is appropriate that I tell you about the treatment of a fistula, which is done through an operation with my hands.
And you should know that fistulas occur in all parts of the body; among them are fistulas that affect major veins and arteries, or nerves, or the spleen, or the bowels, or the bladder, or one of the ribs, or one of the vertebrae of the back, or one of the complex joints such as those of the hand and foot; and some of these are near to the main part of the limb and similar.
And therefore, among these fistulas that follow this method, there are diseases difficult to heal, and they do not easily receive a cure, and the treatment of them requires labor and ignorance.
And if any of the fistulas has a depth which does not reach the places mentioned above, and you hope and trust in its cure, and wish to gain knowledge of it, then take a probe of iron or steel, and if the fistula runs straight ahead, explore it with that.
And if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a probe of lead, for the lightness of lead causes it to float and curve around the tortuosity.
And if the fistula has openings that are frightening, it is not within your power to signify upon them with a probe.
Then anoint the openings through the openings, for the moisture with which you anoint it will go to the other openings and flow from them.
And then inquire very carefully, according to the method by which you can know whether here there is bone or nerve, and whether the depth of the fistula is far or near, or if it is a fistula with all its openings lying on top of everything at the limit of your strength, of your thorough inquiry, of which you inquire about the disease, and the cause of the pain lies under your compression upon the place, and similar signs and accidental causes to the abscess.
And when you have stood upon all those causes with true judgment, then proceed to the cure with confidence: and it is that you consider now whether it is a fistula that is apparent, near, or in a salt place, far from a joint, or nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places which I have told you about.
Now you dry the fistula according to what was said before about its description, and remove what is in that filtration and of corrupted flesh, and what has grown in the opening of the flesh or tissue and similar parts, and treat it until it is healed.
If the fistula is of great depth and runs straight, you should incise it to the primary wound as far as possible, and then remove all the corrupted flesh; afterwards apply bandages wrapped in sharp medicines, and insert them into the cavity of the fistula which you have prepared with iron.
And do this several times so that the sharp medicine dissolves all that is rotten in the cavity of the fistula from corruption.
And then treat it with applications that generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it does not heal with this treatment, then you should cauterize it as we have recommended.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and you must determine this, then you should investigate it and dry it out as we have recommended, without prohibiting any cause related to veins or nerves or a major limb as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed and there is corruption and blackness on it, then you should excise that until all the corruption is removed; afterwards treat it with what promotes flesh growth until it is healed.
And if it does not heal and pus continues to discharge as before, then you will know that you have not fully eradicated the corruption; then uncover it again and carefully examine and clean it thoroughly with the strongest means available, and afterwards treat it.
And if it heals, that is good; but if not, then you will know that the corruption is in the deep parts of the body, which have not been reached, and then in that case all that remains is to lay open the area.
