On the treatment of hemorrhoids and the quality of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in some fleshy members and the space of the abscess is prolonged internally so that there is a collection of pus, and then it breaks and perforates, allowing the pus to be discharged, leaving the place empty as if it were a vessel, and the membrane above it remains thin like cloth, without having reached putrefaction, nor has it affected the bone or nerves or tendons, it is called a hemorrhoid, and it is not named a fistula.
However, if the space is prolonged internally so that putrefaction affects some member, it is then called a fistula and not a hemorrhoid.
The treatment for it is to incise that membrane completely as it revolves, and especially when it is thin like cloth, and ensure that you do not adhere to the place due to its putrefaction.
However, if you expect the membrane to adhere and that the putrefaction has not reached advanced stages, and there is pus present in the membrane, then you should treat it with enemas.
And when you consider the hemorrhoid, if it is large and the limb you are treating has a foul odor, then you should administer an Egyptian medicine as an enema. This medicine consists of oil, vinegar, and camphor in equal parts, which are mixed in a vessel and heated over fire until the mixture becomes thick like honey.
Take the water that is necessary and dissolve in it honey and water; and administer a cleansing enema with it, and strain the opening of it, and leave the medicine inside for the quantity of two hours.
Then extract it in that manner; and do this for several days until the sinus is cleansed and the pus is removed.
And sometimes it is administered with water of ashes when this medicine is not present; and you should take the ash of wheat, put it over the water; then strain that and administer it until you are confident that the sinus is healed.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the water of ashes, then administer it with water and honey mixed three times, and also administer water and honey mixed with vinegar, for the property of honey cleanses and washes, and the vinegar makes the sinus adhere, especially when the vinegar has a surplus of styptic and drying properties.
And as you do this for several days and become confident that the sinus is cleansed, then administer it with water that promotes flesh growth.
And you should take diaphoretic herbs and dissolve them in rose oil and styptic vinegar; or administer it with several medicines and other herbs that have the properties I have already written about in the chapter on herbs.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow and does not receive the enema tube, then gradually enlarge it with a small instrument, and pause with cloth wrapped in Egyptian or basilicon herbs until it is enlarged.
If the opening is wide, then you should suture together the edges and leave a quantity in which the enema may be introduced.
And if the opening from which the arm protrudes is raised to the upper parts, then you should make it dry in the pus base located where the arm can pass below, for when the arm is compressed at the depth of the bend, flesh will be generated there.
And if it is not possible to dry the sinus within the vicinity of the pus base as you wish, then you should perforate it so that you prepare the member in such a way that the arm can pass easily according to your preparation and without any obstruction hindering the arm.
But that which is over the sinus opening should be changed at all times so that it can absorb what is adhering to the arm. And so that you may know whether what comes from the arm is much or little, or is digested or undigested.
Thus you should consider the location of that sinus, whether the patient feels pain there or not, and whether it is in an abscess.
And as your treatment is prolonged in this manner, and you see that the matter lying over the orifice of the sinus has adhered or tends to adhere, then you must loosen it either on the same day or on the third day, and when you loosen the plaque, then change the dressing for another one and renew the application.
But if the sinus extends into spaces where there is thin pus, then do not despair of its adherence, for healing will tend to occur in such cases, just as some people experience delayed regeneration of flesh in their plaques, while others experience the opposite.
And if from the sinus there has been discharged for several days a foul and undigested matter, then you should know that the sinus does not adhere; and if the adherence of the sinus is delayed and becomes prolonged, then adjust to a drying effect in your medicines. If these medicines are moist by their nature and in their virtue, they must be dried as well when applied, especially when there is an excessive addition of honey.
And the last thing to do for those who are cured is to take a finely prepared ointment and dissolve it in rose oil. Let this be applied over an old dressing that contains beneficial substances, and apply it as needed.
And it seems appropriate in that action, when there is no foul matter, to take a finely prepared ointment and honey which has been cooked until thickened, and use it; or take myrrh, aloes, and other ingredients, mix them all together, powder them over the honey while it is on the fire, then apply this mixture over a cloth and strengthen it over the sinus; or spread the honey that you have cooked to thicken over a cloth and sprinkle powdered ingredients over it.
For some wounds, administer a bandage with long and fine threads of celestial lily (which is the celestial lily) and flour of oats and centaury, and use these simples or mix them, tear them up and sieve them, then powder them over the honey which has been recommended; and thus apply it.
And if the sinew is extended and does not heal with this treatment, then you should know that corruption is in its root, and it has already spread to the bones or other hard members or ligaments; now treat it with a fistula treatment as mentioned above.

Of the extraction of the leg vein.
This vein is generated in the thighs in warm lands such as in Al-Ategi in the land of Arabs and in the hot and dry lands of India where no grass or vegetation grows.
And this vein is generated in other parts of the body besides the thighs.
And its formation is from putrefaction that occurs below the skin, just as worms and vermin come forth within the body between the skin and the flesh.
And the sign of the occurrence of this condition is that inflammation occurs violently in the thigh; and nearby, the area becomes blistered; and soon the vein begins to appear from the location of that blister as if it were a plant rash.
And when the extremity of it appears, then you should wrap around that area a piece of lead weighing about two drachmas, and attach the lead to that spot so that it hangs from the thigh.
And so many times when you see some cause for parts outside, wrap that in lead and seal it. And if it is elongated, then you cut a part of it and wrap the remainder; and do not cut that little piece until it has come out, for if you cut that one, it contracts and goes into the flesh and causes an abscess and putrefaction in the place and a bad ulceration.
So it is necessary to treat it gently and extract it little by little until it is completely removed from the rear of it and there remains no cause for it in the body.
And this vein has emerged in some people to the length of five palms and I have been told that one reached twenty palms.
And if it is cut during the time of its treatment, then insert the reed into the tract of it and perforate that one along the body until everything that is in the matter is evacuated, and administer putrefaction of the place with medicines for several days; and afterwards cure that with the treatment of abscesses.

On the section concerning worms generated under the skin and it is called bovine disease.
This illness is called in some lands bovine disease because often among them there is a worm of which one is born between the skin and the flesh, and it moves throughout the body up and down, and it shows itself to the sensation beyond the nerve from member to member until it breaks through the skin and comes out.
Concerning generation from corruption, it is through putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms, serpents, and parasites occur in the belly.
It is feared because of its malice, for when it enters the body and rises to the head, and reaches the heart, at that moment it forcefully ruptures there and causes various illnesses.

Regarding the treatment of nasal disorders.
This illness is called "nafir" in our land, and it is a pain that occurs in certain limbs, and then changes from limb to limb.
And I have already seen that as I will now describe to you. I went to visit a sick woman in a village, and when I uncovered her arm, I saw a small inflammation in the funis vein of the arm, and after it had been punctured for about an hour, I saw that this inflammation moved just like a live worm ascends this way, but more quickly than seems possible, as if it were living silver moving from place to place.
Then the pain moved from that place to the muscle, and it stayed there for about an hour.
And then it quickly moved to the other side of the body until it reached the other arm.
And then he told me that it was moving throughout her body as I had seen; and I was greatly amazed at the change and the speed with which it moved from limb to limb. And I had seen something like this before in my own patients in that manner, but I have never seen the pain change from one limb to another so distinctly. But I did not see this in my patients as I did in the woman.
And I did not think this was possible except that the woman was of delicate constitution, with thin limbs and exposed veins, in which this airiness of sensation appeared to change.
And it is necessary that this does not appear in those who are robust and have healthy flesh with sturdy veins.
And when you want to treat it and the patient feels this pain, immediately apply pressure to that area gently and quickly move over it until this confined airiness has exited, and then cauterize the area.
If you do not use them [the fingers] to feel the swelling, then treat it with a decoction of the body and with what expels wind, and charm those nasty pills and those of serpentium and similar.
Of the extraction of thorns.
Thorns are diverse according to their species, and according to the parts of the body in which they lodge, for their diversity is such that some have large points, others small, some concave, others blunt; and among them are those with three angles, and those which have tongues; and there are those which are like slender rods.
And the signs of the brain are when you catch the thorn, it penetrates the bone, and exits at the cephalic point which is above the brain, for it causes sudden fever, scotoma, vertigo, rigidity in the fingers, inflammation and heat of the tongue, spasms and confusion of understanding, vomiting of bile, and often speech is cut off and the voice destroyed. Moisture like white powder comes out from the location of the wound, and its appearance resembles a watery consistency of flesh.
And therefore if I show you these signs, then you abstain from treatment and the extraction of the arrow, unless it is outside.
Mark the signs of the arrow when it hits the heart, so that it is near the left nipple, and feel there where it is fixed in a hard part, not in a soft part; and the movement of the arrow is similar to the movement of the pulse, and there flows black blood from the wound, and the extremities become cold, and sweat turns cold, and syncope occurs; therefore you know that death will come very soon.
And the signs when the arrow has pierced the lung are foam of blood from the wound, and the veins near the neck are swollen, and the complexion is changed, and he exhales a great breath, and demands for air with difficulty.
And if the arrow hits the membrane that is in the chest, and the pain then is near the small ribs, and it causes great and violent pain and sighing, and there is no movement at all of the limbs by the muscles.
And if the arrow hits the stomach, then very soon the wound vomits some undigested food.
And if the arrow hits the abdomen, and it is fixed there, some part of the wound splits open, or the membrane, or intestine which is ruptured, then there is no hope in the treatment or extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow hits the bladder, and urine is discharged, and some cause of injury spreads to other parts, and very soon there is pain over the body, then you know that it is fatal.
Like other members also as in the face, the neck, and the groin, and the shoulders and the helpers, and the vertebrae of the back, and the collarbone, and the chest bone and things like them of the members, they are saved according to the major dispositions when the arrow does not find vein nor artery and is not a poisonous arrow.
And I will recount this to you as I have seen regarding the nature of these arrows, so that you may understand about their treatment.
The reason for this is that when an arrow was embedded in the lacrimal bone at the root of his nose, I extracted it from him on the other side, left the granules behind in his ear, and he healed without any harm coming to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which had embedded in him in the gray matter of his eye, leaving the eyelid behind, and the arrow was already hidden and could not be completely removed except for a small part to which it adhered to its shaft. It was a large sharp arrow of those used by Turkish soldiers, with iron like a lion's head but without ears.
And the Jew recovered, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man who had it embedded in his groin, and it was an Arabic arrow, which had ears; then I incised around that between two fingers, and it was already submerged in his groin; then I carefully worked on it, and soon afterward I removed it, and the Christian recovered.

And it is in the words of men a slight swelling; and if you find that, and it does not appear to the sense of all, then phlebotomize some branch of it, and that is which appears on the two of the hand behind the finger auricular and the finger anular.
And beware of the nerves, and let the blood flow in the quantity that you wish.
And then loosen the bandage and pause on the place of the phlebotomy to compress the place, for it is quickly healed.
Of venotoma and of the qualities of using those.
Venotomies are usually made from horns, or from earth, or from flint.
And venotoma is done according to two methods: one of them is as scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is venotoma without scarification.
And this venotoma which is without scarification is according to two methods, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And the venotomies that are usually administered with scarification and extraction of blood are fourteen places on the body, of which one where venotomies are placed is alnocrati, and it is at the back part of the head; and another middle place between two parts of the neck; and venotomies under the chin below the jaw near the mouth; and venotomies of the two cheeks; and venotomies of alohoster, which is above the collarbone; and venotomies of two armpits; and venotomies of two thighs; and venotomies of two feet.
From the swelling you do not draw blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and through this you do not catch the virtue through those [veins], just as it is done by phlebotomy; nor does it matter whether venesection be performed near a fire or away from fire in any disease caused by retention until all that is within the body is evacuated.
If necessity causes venesection due to illness or for regular maintenance, administer that at any time at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, in the middle of the location and at whatever time may be appropriate.
And when you encounter any of those causes, and particularly if it is in the third or middle of the month, I command that venesection be performed after two or three hours have passed from noon.
The benefit of venesection from the neck is that when it relieves heaviness in the head and what causes sinking in the eyes; but it must be done near complete evacuation of the entire body.
And this vein is in the place of phlebotomy of the cephalic region.
And it is not good to use that which is of full friction, or in which there is a cold, because it gives great harm to the patient.
And for this reason, it is not advisable to use those old veins, and in many cases, they cause fragile illnesses.
But for the one who is continuously having it done on them, memory loss may occur; and therefore, you should instruct the practitioner to pause their hand briefly when removing the cupping vessel from the area, in order to prevent the generation of forgetfulness.
But cupping on the nape is in the place of phlebotomy for the dark vein and the basilic vein: and thus it benefits asthma, constriction of breath, splitting pain in the chest, coughing, and congestion.
And it is necessary to lift the cupping vessel on the nape slightly, for if it is left below too long, it generates weakness in the body and in the stomach.
And cupping on the back of the head benefits accidental pains in the head and ophthalmia, migraine, nasal congestion, and pain in the roots of the teeth; and it is in the place of phlebotomy for the basilic vein.
And you should instruct the cupping practitioner not to press their hand during incision so as not to injure the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And cupping below the chin benefits alcohol-related issues in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and resembles treatments for similar illnesses that occur in the mouth, and it is in the place of phlebotomy of the alheric vein which is on both lips.
Avoid the windiness that is made over the two spatulas by applying a compress, which is made by an abundance of heat.

And command the windworker not to proceed with incision, because the area is bare of flesh, and leave it alone where there are nerves and arteries.
And it is a windiness on which the treatment is applied over the hemorrhoids of the anus and to the ulcers that are nearby.

Avoid windinesses on small areas because they draw blood from the entire body and can cause pain in old wounds of the ribs and chest, and bladder issues; they also provoke menstruation and can lead to boils and abscesses. They should be used instead of phlebotomy from two saphenas, alahorcoben, and two cavilhas.
However, they weaken the entire body and can cause fainting in many people.

Regarding the quality of the position of the cupping vessels, place the vessel at the beginning and suck it gently and appropriately, and do not prolong the position of the cupping vessels, but place them quickly, and remove them carefully, so that the humors come to the site by equal distribution. And do not stop repeating this and continue until you see the area turn red and swell, and the firmness of the blood becomes apparent.
Now you should make incisions and repeat the suction gradually, and then observe the disposition of the body.
For a person with tender flesh and few pores should be scarified only once, not pressed repeatedly, so that the area does not become ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to enlarge the scarification slightly and prolong it, and perform the suction with ease and moderation.
And if the blood is thick, then it is necessary to scarify twice, because in the first session the pathway for the refinement of the blood and its moisture is opened. But in the second session, use the acquired precision for the extraction of thick blood.
And if the blood is very impure, then perform the scarification for a third time so that it achieves the desired result.
And finally, when we want to extract the blood gradually or in small amounts, one scarification is sufficient for us.
And if we want to extract a large amount of blood, we perform extensive scarification; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary to scarify more extensively.
Regarding the term of incision, in which the depth of the wound is concerned only.

And it is necessary that the cupping be done before bathing; preferably it should be administered shortly after bathing by one hour or two, and it is necessary that he sleeps near the cupping.

Chapter XC VII.
Bloodletting should not be administered in the joints but in those members where it is impossible to apply cupping due to their small size, such as the gums and palate and similar areas; or on parts of the body devoid of flesh, such as the fingers and nose and similar areas.
And the quality of using those leeches that you have found should be in the waters below clean and wholesome places.
And then they should be left in clean water day and night until they are hungry, so that there is no remaining cause inside them; and then evacuate the body at the beginning of phlebotomy and cupping; and then rub the member of the patient until it reddens; and then place them on the area.
And when they are full and satisfied.
And if it is possible to suction the area with cupping, it is better for relief.
And if not, then wash the area with vinegar and then with clean water; and it should be rubbed and pressed.
And if the blood flow extends around the site where the leeches were applied and there is any residue, then place a piece of linen in cold water and apply it until the residue is absorbed.
And if the blood continues to flow, then sprinkle vitriol powder or other astringent powders on it until the bleeding stops; or apply crushed fava beans to the area and leave them until they adhere to the site, at which point the bleeding will be stopped.
In case it is necessary, repeat the application of leeches, when possible replace those.
And if the leeches are prohibited from suspending, then at that time wash the area with fresh blood or insert a needle into the spots to let blood; and afterwards they should be paused, for those when feeling any blood will immediately react.
And when you want them to stop, sprinkle a little powdered aloes, or salt, or ash on them, and they will stop immediately.
Thus begins the chapter of the third part: on restoration. This chapter is considered necessary in the art of medicine; it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations, accidents involving the bones.
Oh, son, be aware that those who boast about this chapter are foolish doctors and the unlearned, and those who for any length of time have not regarded the books of the Ancients nor read a single letter from them.
And for this reason it is done that this method of the scientists in our land is private; and I have not forgotten any of it for any length of time; and I have not adopted what follows without extensive reading of the books of the Ancients and studying to understand them, extracting the science from them.
And thus I directed my efforts toward experimentation and study throughout the duration of my life.
And I have already described in this chapter everything that my science and experience contain; now I condense this for you to spare you the burden of lengthiness; and abbreviate this at the end of abbreviation; and demonstrate this at the end of explanation; and present various forms of instruments used in it, as is done by addition of explanation, just as I have done in the two preceding chapters.
First chapter.
A summary and collection of the essentials concerning fractures of bones, from which a preliminary understanding is necessary before we begin the recollection of the parts of fractured limbs and their various treatments. Therefore, let us proceed with the prologue of this chapter by summarizing the essence of the discourse. The chapters that follow will require the craftsman and student to stand firm in the truth of these matters; thus, you and those like you who wish to know or learn this noble art must be prepared.
And therefore, I say that when it happens to someone to suffer a fracture, dislocation, twist, or fall, at that moment it is necessary for you to begin either with phlebotomy or bandaging the injured person, or both simultaneously, unless some cause prohibits this, such as weakness of constitution, if the one who has been affected is an infant, old and decrepit, or if it is a time of extreme heat or intense cold.
And therefore, let the patient be content with food that is light, such as fresh herbs and the meat of birds and small animals; we prohibit heavy meats and foods that fill the body excessively, and all foods that cause the veins to swell with blood, until you are assured of the safety from abscesses, and if you do not fear an effusion of matter into the limb.
And now return the patient to their usual regimen that they were accustomed to follow.
And as the broken bone begins to be restored, it is then necessary for the patient to be fed with nutritious foods that are rich and heavy in consistency, such as those made from boiled wheat with water, rice, beans, cow's stomachs, eggs, fresh meat, and similar items. With this regimen, the healing of the fracture occurs more easily and better.
And know that the bones in children and young people and in the elderly cannot be set or consolidated according to their nature by drying out the limbs.
But nature adjusts and applies them over the broken bone from all parts for some cause similar to glue, in which there is a thickness that adheres and strengthens it as they are conjoined together, until they come to an end of strength and firmness, just as fire at the beginning, without any cause prohibiting it from performing its works.
And for this reason it is necessary that the patient abstains from foods that are heavy, viscous, and thick, as we have said.
And know that the kinds of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the bone of the skull is different from a fracture of the bone of the arm; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the leg; and similarly all other members are different among themselves.
And I wish to provide a recapitulation of every kind of fracture, explained in its own separate chapter.
The variety of fractures within themselves. And there are diverse types of fractures in the bones; for one kind of curved fracture is different from those that occur in the stars; and some are made according to the length of the bone: and so the fractures of the skull and star-shaped and non-star-shaped, just as it is done when a pen is prepared to write, and there is a fracture with a stripe and disruption on the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And each kind has its own torment proper in the restoration of the second, which will come as a reminder of the distinct ones in their places.
Mark the signs of a bone fracture. And by this, a bone fracture is known: it is the twisting of the bone, and from its prominence and appearance to the touch, and also when you press on it with your hand.
And therefore when there is no apparent twisting in its place or sound, and it does not cause significant pain upon gentle touch when you touch the bone with movement and the patient does not feel great pain, then there is no fracture here.
There might be twisting or a small fissure, so do not press on it with your hand and by compression of all means; instead, apply one of the medicines about which there is a reminder at that moment when you deem it necessary for the location; and it may be tight due to subtle constriction.
And know that the place where it is curved and not split into two or more parts as happens in the skull, as is described throughout its comparison, now you should begin from your time to correct it whenever an abscess occurs.
And if an abscess occurs in the wound, immediately do not delay it for any days until the abscess ceases; and then clean it according to each method that you can by lightness and ingenuity.
And know that the restoration of it and alignment of it is easier than that of the bone in which such a fracture occurs.
And then strengthen it according to what will come to your recollection.
But if the bone is fractured, then it is necessary to extend this broken limb from both ends, whether it is the hand or the foot; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two ropes or one rope and your hand.
And place yourself in such a position that you can put the limb in a proper position according to its natural figure, while the parts that are extended from the broken member, then force yourself to return those adductions with skill and according to each method that you can into their places.
And be careful according to your strength that no harm comes to the patient because of your operation or injury; and strive according to your ability that you apply one bone to its companion bone according to their best forms.
And it is necessary that at that opportune moment you apply your hand effectively, and if you encounter any different cause, correct that and adjust it according to the extent of your virtue.
And avoid excessive extension and compression, just as some foolish surgeons often cause an abscess in the wound by their operation, or cause contraction in the limb as we have seen from their misguided actions.
And the quality of the constriction of the calm limb is what I recommend; for you to know that broken limbs vary in their smallness and largeness and in the form of those.
And for those that are small such as the arm and the hand and the finger and similar to these, it is necessary that the wrapping be of soft fine cloth.
But for those that are large such as the thigh or the chest, it is necessary that their wrapping be leather and hard, because the leather binding connects the large limb and strengthens it on all sides with equal constriction, thus preventing any injury.
And then begin, after the proper evacuation of the constriction, to pause conveniently for this, on the site of the fracture with three wrappings or four according to what the limb requires; and tighten your hand slightly with the bandage.
And then go to the upper part of the most prominent site of the fracture, and tighten moderately so that your constriction does not hurt the place where you have tightened the fracture. From around the fracture, extend the bandage a little, and gradually loosen the tightness, until you take from it a large part.
Take another bandage and wrap it again around the fractured area with the wrapping, and then bring the wrappings down below the fracture; and your procedure should be in tightening the bandaging and loosening it as we have said about the first wrapping above.
Then pause between the wrappings with a piece of linen or cloth equal to the curvature of the fracture if there is curvature; and if not, do not pause for any reason.
Then pause on the other bandage, and then apply plasters properly made immediately if there is no inflammation or abscess in the area, and at that time pause where the abscess is and where the inflammation is; and leave it like this for several days; and then you will strengthen it with the plasters.
And let the plasters be half a cubit wide in length, and well formed wisely; or let them be made of pine wood; or from palm leaves; and let the width of each plaster be according to this form and this figure:
However, the plaster that is placed over the fracture should be slightly thicker and longer than the others, and the length of the plaster according to how it fits the limb in size; then reinforce the plasters with another bandage as per your initial tightening; then tie it above properly as we have said about tightening, and it should be your tightening most near the fracture site more tightly, and the further away from the fracture, the less tight it should be.
And let the threads be medium in thickness and fineness, and let them be soft; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen some foolish people use cords made of hair for their threads, it is a great mistake, when one considers the need for equality in tension.
And similarly, very fine threads are not suitable either, for you will not achieve the desired tension with them, and it is necessary that there be at least one digit between each needle.
And if the patient complains of pain at the ends of the threads near the ligature in those places, then pause and use thread made of lion's wool or carded lamb's wool until it does not cause injury.
Sometimes a fracture occurs due to a blow, and there is disruption in the bone, and I will recall this case in that chapter in the second section.
And know that not every broken member should be set with threads on the first day; the reason for this is that when the member is large, it should not be set with threads but after about five or seven days, or more, according to your certainty regarding the danger of an abscess.
And this is the method of plasters with which the Ancients treated fractures, dislocations, and twists, which were applied over injuries.
Method of making a plaster for the quick restoration of fractures, suitable for various conditions and especially for women and children, as it does not generate heat or irritation: take powdered flour, which is the fine residue that settles on the walls of the mill after grinding.
And confine it as it is, but sift it through white cloth, and stop the compounding of it not thin nor subtle; and then use it.
Another method for plaster to restore fractures, dislocations, and twists: recipe of asafetida, of cerate, of acacia, of inula, and migraach, which is the rind of wild millet, of each one drachm; of myrrh and aloes each one half drachm; alochil two drachms; all should be thrice sifted and mixed with water or with white cloth if the composition is hot; and then use this plaster, for it is good composition and restores broken bones quickly, and confine the patient to movement.
Another method for a plaster to treat fractures and twists: recipe of migraach and root of white mullein one drachm; myrrh and aloes each half drachm; acacia six drachms; Armenian bole two drachms.
All should be thrice triturated in a mortar with a concave shape, and should be sifted, and should be compounded with water or white cloth if the composition is hot, and it should be administered.
Method for plaster for joints and bones that have moved from their places, and for accidental pain in them and for splits and fractures: take pure wool and immerse it in sour wine and hot oil and apply it to the location.
Moreover, in this plaster there is no virtue of restoration, but it is good for treating abscesses and properly expelling pains.
Another method for a plaster that restores a broken bone: take fresh leaves of the fig tree and leaves of wild poppy; crush them together and make a plaster from them.
Method of preparing a quick plaster for administration during the restoration of a bone fracture and when you want resolution of the pus abscess: take rhizomes of elder, of chamomile and flowers of violets, of flour of spelt, of each one part; mix all together and boil with wine, if the member is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, now mix that with cold coriander water or with water and use it.
Another method of making a very strong plaster for resolution compared to the first one, when there is a hard abscess during the restoration of the limb: take rhizomes of elder and seeds of linseed, and pomegranate flowers, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, of each one equal part; mix all together and boil with savory water or with gentle water or with wine, whichever suits the condition of the limb and according to its repose by its color.
But Hippocrates does not mention in his book that it should be paused on the broken limb during its restoration other than wax made of beeswax and oil, and he recommends that it be moderate between coarseness and fineness.
But in Wales, it is observed that they are applied to the broken limb during its restoration and for causes where there is a slight drying with warmth as well as substances like myrrh, onions, and similar to those causes which we have recommended.
Method of preparing a plaster to relieve injury and pain: take pitch, linseed, human hair cut or bird's feathers, and elder, and salt, equal parts; mix all together, sift them, and boil, then make into a plaster.
The quantity according to which it is necessary for the bandage to remain and then be loosened is that you consider whether pain does not occur or improvement does not take place nor is the bone out of its place; therefore, do not loosen it for several days. And if intense pain or improvement or inflammation occurs at the site, then you should be cautious or loosen it at that hour, and remove the plaster from there.
And take linen cloth and a sponge soaked in seawater, and immerse it in fresh water, then use it to wash the area until the inflammation subsides and the pain is alleviated; afterward let the limb rest for an hour; then place it on wool saturated with vinegar and oil or scented oil, and bind it there overnight until you are sure that no abscess has formed, and until the swelling of the limb has subsided, and the pain from it is relieved.
And afterward, repeat that gentle bandaging and small plaster without tightening it as much as the first time, and gently do this until it is healed.
And if you find that the abscess, swelling, pain, and inflammation have all subsided completely, and you need a plaster and bandaging, then repeat that as you did at the beginning, similarly.
And if no cause arises for the limb of those we have mentioned, then do not loosen it until after three or four or ten or seven days; and in some cases all of this is left for twenty days, as you deem appropriate by loosening the bandage, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and the flesh coagulates over it.
At this time adjust the tightening more than in your first stricture entirely, and adjust according to the thickening of the patient's flesh, as was preceded by the recollection of it.
But if you see the site of the fracture already drying and becoming more exposed than is necessary, then you will know that nutrition is prohibited from reaching it; and therefore apply to it a plaster with vinegar which you should always replace whenever it dries out; and do this for all three days; and loosen the bandage slightly, and by this operation direct nutrition to the member, and it is healed quickly.
But what those foolish restorers of the fractured member do otherwise when it is not restored at the beginning as necessary, and is restored according to its tortuosity, is an error according to their operation, and great fear, and if there were a judge, this would be greatly enlarged in the Ancients' books and recognized as such.
And I have not seen any of them do this, which is why it is more reasonable that they should not do so.
Chapter second: On fracture when it occurs in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by the impact of an object, and the impact removes all the bone within the skull, which is behind the bone, just as it would in wood, and thus this type is named a schiña; or the object incises some part of the bone, and cuts into the flesh but does not penetrate beyond that point; and this species of fracture is called absolute elevation.
This is about the injury of these two fractures, either it is great or small, and of the fracture that is comminuted or crushed; and the cause of them is impact with poor ground or falling on stone, and things similar to this.
And this fracture is penetrating, which approaches the peritoneum below the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the injury of these two fractures is either great or small.
And of the fracture that is weakened in the bone, in the subtlety of the small hair-like structures, and for this reason this particular type of fracture is named capillary.
And it is a fracture that is caused either by falling or by the impact of a stone and similar actions, which causes some fragments of the bone to enter into the soft tissues, and it occurs in the concave location just as it happens with ribs when they are impacted and broken inwardly into the surrounding tissues.
And this is more commonly caused in the bones of children's bodies, just as it happens with the heads of infants.
And for all these types of fractures, treatments are used, which are remedies applied separately. And I will return to the recall of all this in its place.
And you will recognize all these types of fractures by detection on them and by inquiry into their symptoms and the appearance of damaged flesh that is over them.
And the capillary fracture is recognized by this: it appears on the bone, and when it is cleaned and examined with a probe, the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of fracture consider at the beginning the symptoms of the patient, and if you see by the symptoms that it signifies clearly from fear, as is vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of understanding, and hoarseness of voice, and fainting, and acute fever, and such like of the symptoms in the major dispositions, without doubt it is not healed.
And if you see unusual symptoms that give hope of health, then you begin the treatment of it.
The cause of which is known when it comes to you as a condition in the beginning as it is a condition and this is on the days of winter, then it is necessary that you study the nature of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all dispositions.
And if it is on the days of summer, then it is necessary that you expose the bone before the seventh day, knowing that what is around the bone may become corrupted by pus, and that symptoms which we have recounted may occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the dura mater covering the brain and is in small pieces and abrasions, then it is necessary that you excise the comminuted and abraded part, as I shall relate to you.
And it is that you shave the head of the patient who is afflicted, and uncover the bone according to custom and method as best you can according to the shape of the injury, and that it be clear on the patient.
And if it happens to you that the detection of the bone, bleeding, or abscess requires caution, then you must proceed with what is necessary, which is to apply the area with cloths infused in wine and rose oil, and let the wound rest until the abscess is subdued, and you are sure there is no further bleeding; then begin the circumvention of the bone and its removal.
In this way it should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you incise the bone with an incisor of such a shape, and this is its form: And then apply another incisor, this one slightly wider than the previous one; and this is its form:
And then apply an even wider incisor than the second one.
And it is necessary to have several different incisors, some of which are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than the others, and their tips should be sharp, made of iron or steel.
And use them with ease during percussion on the incisors so that you do not damage the head and tip of them.
And if the bone is hard and strong, it is necessary to drill around it before you apply the incisors with the chisels, which are called non-profound chisels.
And they are called this because these do not reach the depth of the bone of the head as far as what is adjacent to it; therefore, the chisel has a rounded tip or margin above which is like a small circle or margin that prevents it from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take many of these chisels, each of which should be suitable for the thickness of each bone, so that you have present in all parts of the head an end that is sharp, with a quantity in length and shortness according to the thickness of this part.
This is the form of three kinds of probes: The quality of piercing around the broken bone is that you place the probe on the bone and revolve it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated; then move the probe to another spot, and insert it throughout to a depth equal to the thickness of the shaft or about there.
And then incise with an instrument what is between each piece of the bone, and make that pus as fine as you can at the end for lightness, as we have said, until you eradicate the bone, or by your hand, or by some other cause through disturbances which you will have prepared for this purpose, such as probes and fine forceps.
And it is necessary that you guard with all caution that the incisor does not touch any part of the wound.
And so when you have perforated around the bone, and it will be separated from the wound if it is continuous there, and you will extract that, then rasp and smooth that in the roughness of the place where it is in the bone with another tool similar to an incisor, but it must be softer and finer than other incisors.
And if any part of the small broken bones remains and you are struggling with it, now take that with ease using what is at hand among your tools; and then treat the wound with lint and dressings as we have mentioned.
And the other method of operation is light, distant from fear, which Guilla and the lauzec praise greatly; and this is the speech about it: It is necessary for you at the beginning to start uncovering the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe and obvious, until when you have uncovered that part and you have placed below it the extremity of this slow and cheerful incisor, which has this form:
And so the lens-shaped part is solid and does not divide for any cause, and the sharp part divides it into two parts lengthwise, as often happens; that the lens-shaped part fit to the suture with the dividing surface sharp on the bone.
And therefore, you strike upon the sutures of a part with a small hammer so that you divide all the bone easily as it is revolved, and you be sure of the periosteum so that no accident happens in any way, and if the surgeon were the most foolish of all men and the least knowledgeable among them; certainly, and if he were asleep.
Now if there is any cause attached to the bone of the periosteum in some places of bone, it will divide that which is near the end of the lens-shaped suture, and it will separate easily, for it separates without harm or fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the periosteum, and that which is separated or remains of the bone is its surface, and it remains in roughness and small protrusions, now you should shave off that roughness and eradicate those protrusions with fine razors for which you have prepared different quantities so that you can administer those most suitable for each location and most appropriate according to how your operation leads on that broken bone and the shape of it.
And you should administer according to your proportion at which you shave the bone initially with the widest of those razors, and then gradually use the finer ones, and do not cease doing this continuously until you reach administering the thinnest and finest of all of them.
But in each of the other small, hairline fractures and subtle fractures, you should administer care according to what is needed for the correction of them.
And it is not surprising that they are not amazed by all those who have studied in that art. And this concerns what we have written about the great fractures.
And if the fat is rectangular when you remove the bone, then cover it with a piece of cloth according to the size of the wound, and immerse that in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound; and then take another folded or triple-layered piece of cloth and immerse it in vinegar and rose oil, and place it on the first cloth, doing this as gently as possible so as not to disturb the tissue; and then apply a linen bandage, but do not wrap it tightly except just enough to cover the wound.
And leave it like this for one day or two until you are sure of the condition of the abscess; and then untie it and administer medicines that relieve excessive dryness, such as linseed poultice and flour of oats, and powder of vetches and of aristology, and similar remedies.
And it is that you should make these medicines into a powder that is finely ground and applied to the wound when they are dry; and generally, administer any medicine whose property is to cleanse without irritating.
And act according to your virtue so that the wound in your treatment remains clean; do not let it become infected or worsen; and do not allow its virulence to increase. For when it is properly applied to the fat of the brain, it corrupts it, weakens it, and leads to further illness due to this malevolent condition.
And it affects the tissue of the head, as when the bone is cleansed and especially when the treatment of it is postponed, there appears a blackness on its surface; and therefore consider whether it leads to the illnesses we have mentioned, for then you should be wary without any doubt.
And if the blackness does not come from medicine that was applied above it, but rather due to the virtue of that medicine which caused the blackness, then you should take one part honey and three parts olive oil, and with these anoint a cloth, and immediately apply that over the wound; and afterward treat it with the spices appropriate for healing until it is healed.
Chapter III.
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken into more than two main parts, or occasionally just one of them, because there are two bones in it; the lower part is cartilaginous and does not break, but only becomes bruised, swollen, and distorted.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then you should insert your little finger into the side of the nose, and adjust that fracture with your finger inside, and hold it in place externally until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this with ease, and be careful that your operation does not cause pain to the patient.
And if the fracture is in the uppermost part of the nose, and your finger cannot reach it, then you should use the tip of a probe with some thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you should proceed as described above.
And attempt the restoration of it on the first day of the fracture if possible; and if not, then wait until the seventh or tenth day when the swelling has subsided.
Approach the end of the nose with a piece of linen cloth if the fracture is in one piece, or use two pieces if the fracture is in two parts, and let each piece be thick enough to fill the groove of the nose.
Some of the ancient restorers say that the cloths should be dipped in butter and changed every day, but I have not seen this done; however, it is advisable to dip the cloth in egg whites mixed with powdered sugar, and then place the cloth on until the bone is set and the cartilage has hardened.
And in some cases, a piece of leather wrapped in linen should be inserted into the nose instead of cloths, if the fracture of the nose is more severe and the patient is not prohibited from breathing through it.
And this method is not necessary for you; if you prefer, do this, and if you prefer, use cloths.
And if another situation arises requiring cauterization, apply a plaster to the nose with pitch or wax dipped in vinegar and rose oil, or with cerate.
But if cauterization is not necessary, then you should apply a plaster outside with sesame flour and powdered herbs mixed with egg whites; place it over a piece of linen, and do not cover the nose with anything.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or crushed, then you should cut around them and extract them using instruments suitable for that purpose; then close the gap and treat it with substances that promote healing and consolidation, and use appropriate plasters.
And if it happens that plague befalls the nose, now you must treat it with poultices and administer lead tubes inside until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
On the restoration of the jaw when it is dislocated.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture does not have inflammation, observe now whether the fracture is only outwardly displaced, and it is not broken into two pieces and curved inward, then its diagnosis is easy.
And what you must do, if the fracture is on the right side, is to insert your left thumb into the mouth of the patient; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert your right thumb; and press with it inside the swelling of the fracture outwardly with ease using your other hand from outside the bone; do this gently.
And if the jaw fracture is already separated into two parts, then you must administer extension of both parts according to straightness as much as possible until they are aligned.
And if it happens that the teeth are knocked out due to a disruption, then strengthen those of which you expect them to break with a thread of gold or silver or silk; and immediately apply a cloth on the broken jaw, and then place it on a doubled cloth, and place on it a bandage properly made, or a piece of softened leather equal in length to the jaw; and then tie it above as is convenient for you to secure the binding and appropriate application, so that it is not destroyed.
And he commands the patient to use rest and tranquility, and to pause from eating liquid foods.
And if you find that the shape has already been altered in any way, then on the third day you should apply heat and loosen it; and afterward, correct what has already been altered with powder of myrtle, egg white, or flour of chickpeas, after you have removed the cerate from it, and place over the dressing a piece of linen.
And then when it is attached and adjusted to the plaster on it, and the position of the bone has not been altered, leave it so that you do not loosen it until it is healed and the fracture is strengthened; for in about three weeks this fracture usually becomes strong.
And if an abscess occurs at another time, then administer what we have said should be done during the treatment until it is healed, and allow that abscess to drain.
And if the fracture is with a splinter, then consider whether it is a piece of bone separated or stuck, and you should carefully remove those splinters with what is appropriate for removing them using the instruments.
And if the opening of the splinter is narrow, then you should enlarge it with a scalpel according to the amount necessary for your needs.
And then when you have removed those splinters, and none of them will drain by itself, close the opening of the splinter if it is wide; and if not, place one of the dressings that are appropriate for this on it to promote healing until it is healed.
Chapter 4
On the restoration of the collarbone when it is fractured.
Most often the collarbone is fractured in the part in front of the muscle, and that fracture is made in one of three ways.
For it is either broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without the occurrence of displacement; and it is easy for its restoration. Or it happens with displacement, and its restoration is difficult; or it happens that the fracture is along the surface.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is along the surface, is that you should have two assistants present, so that one holds the splint and supports the broken collarbone, and the other extends the arm to that part.
And then mold the fracture with the fingers so that it is shaped as needed, and there should be neither a small prominence nor a depression.
And if you need more extension, then place under the armpit of the injured side a pad made of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to your necessity; extend and elevate the collarbone and compress the pad with your hand so that you can mold the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the end of the collarbone to the outer parts, so that it reaches the clavicle, then have the patient lie on their shoulder, and place under the muscle a pad about half as large.
And the assistant presses the muscle below so that the bone of the collarbone is elevated to meet the clavicle, and then you should correct the fracture and mold it with your fingers as needed.
If you feel pain from the furcula and its maladies, now you must cut into it and extract those pains with ease.
And if it is retained in the bone, now you must devise an incision with one of the instruments that you have prepared for this purpose, since you paused below the furcula the tool that serves as a pick; and this tool is made of wood or iron and has the following form:
And it is similar to a bowl of which this is the concavity; and its width should be according to what you need from it, depending on the size of the bone or the scarcity of the space.
But the length of it should be according to what is possible for the operation; let there be two ends as you see fit, and one end should be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound that you have incised when extracting the pain from the bone is wide, and you are sure it will form an abscess, now you must bring the two lips of the wound together with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear it may form an abscess, now you should treat the wound with plasters and powders according to the quantity of the wound's incision.
And if an abscess does occur, now apply a plaster made of green vegetables and rose oil mixed with vinegar, and place it over the area.
And do not let the splint press down on the broken bone; and therefore it is necessary that you see the patient every day, so that when the bandage has loosened or you see the splint already moved, then you must correct this and tighten the binding.
And place the patient's pillow on their neck, and place beneath it a small roll to elevate the support of their back, so that when the fracture of the collarbone is elevated by the muscles, or the arm is bandaged to the neck.
And do not loosen the bandage unless an abscess occurs in the vicinity or within xii days.
And then renew the plaster if you see that the face of it needs it, and adjust it to the constriction and restore it until it is restored and the collarbone fracture is healed.
And it is tightened and comforted according to the measure in xxviii days, and in some cases it is done in less time for some people.
Chapter VI.
On the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade is broken in the middle and not shattered but only cracked at the edges.
And when it is broken in that place — and it is broken in the middle — and it is known only by touch, then according to the figure of the fracture prepare yourself and reduce it according to its natural shape in every way that is possible for you.
And then pause over the place where you sprinkle powdered egg white and stop there; and then pause over the doubled cloth; and then pause over the sieve, taking a small amount of powder according to the quantity of the spoon all or at most a little.
But if you leave the sieve with a hollow in some part of the spoon, then you fill that hollow with cloth and then press the sieve according to equality.
And then apply pressure above with a metallic hook of appropriate length and with firmness to the sieve so that it does not move from its place.
And examine the bandage each day; and every time it is softened, change it on another day and tighten it and adjust the sieve if it has moved from its place.
And place the dressing on its side as is appropriate.
And the spoon is restored in xx days or in .xxv.
When this number is complete, then you dissolve the bandage, and you know for certain which are the bones of which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed with the piece of bone and place the chain underneath it, then you cut above it, remove it, and do as we have said regarding the fracture of the collarbone or abscess cauterization if this occurs here.
Chapter VII.
On the restoration of a foot fracture.
The foot sometimes is broken in the middle and remains there; but the ends move as cicadas do, moving separately and apart.
And of the conditions when the middle of it is broken, it is that it inclines towards the internal parts and there occurs great pain, difficulty in movement, coughing, fever, vomiting blood; and it is evident in the cavity of the broken bone and not hidden from sensation.
And the restoration of it is that the patient be laid supine on their bed, and place a cushion between their shoulders; then gently press the muscles, and adjust the ribs by bringing together more than two ribs, and carefully perform the operation and adjustment in every possible way suitable for this, until the figure of the bone returns as required.
And then place over it a plaster and cotton, and place beneath it thin splints of wood or reeds or something similar in lightness, after which you wrap that in cloth; then gently bind that over the broken bone without putting too much pressure; and go around the binding several times along the back, and tighten that with appropriate firmness; and then examine the bandage at all times, and when it becomes loose, tighten it.
And if necessity compels the removal of that which has been corroded or protruded or caused pain or an abscess in the area, then you should cut and loosen it and remove the plaster and correct what happened with this method of treatment; and immediately replace the plaster if you have to do so for that part; and adjust the tightness until it is healed.
Chapter VIII.
On the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in thick parts where they meet; and at their extremities in front, such fractures do not happen because those parts are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden to the sense of touch during the examination of the aforementioned parts.
And the restoration should be such that it equalizes the fracture of the aforementioned parts according to the possible method, until it is set as needed, and then apply plaster and bind the broken bone with a splint if necessary.
And if the fracture of the ribs inclines towards the inner parts, then severe pain and piercing occur, just as in the case of one who has wept, and because of this, the bone puts pressure on the membrane; and it leads to difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood; and this is difficult to treat.
And the Ancients were skilled in that with powerful remedies.
And what they say is: it is necessary to avoid foods that cause severe inflammation and gas, so that the abdomen becomes inflated and extended, and the fracture is pushed outward.
Let us proceed with what is necessary to avoid causing unnecessary harm that might lead to the formation of an abscess by cautery, if it does not already exist; and if it has already occurred, now help with this.
And some of them say that it should be placed above the wound and near where it draws up strength; and this is similar to reasoning, but it is feared that the wound may draw the superfluities to the place due to its weakness.
And some of them say: cover the place with wool that has already been submerged in hot oil, and place poultices between the ribs until it is filled, so that the bandage fits according to its roundness; and treat the wound as you would for the cure of pleurisy with food and medicine.
And if the wound is caused by violent constriction that cannot be tolerated, and the bone pierces through the membrane at a point, we fear for the wound; now it should be necessary to cut above the location, expose the broken rib, and then place an instrument that preserves the safety previously determined, and with ease, incise the bone and extract it.
And then we adjust the two edges of the wound, if it is large, by stitching, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs internally, now you should incise it and apply poultices in rose oil on the area, and treat the wound with what is appropriate for an internal abscess.
And let them lie supine on the side on which they sleep until they are healed.
Chapter IX.
On the restoration of fractures of the cervical vertebrae and the collarbone.
In some cases, fractures occur in the bones of the collar, and treatment for them is similarly applied to the vertebrae of the spine. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if they will recover or not, at that moment consider: if you see their limbs already softened, numb, and dead, and they cannot move or stretch them, and when you press on those areas with your fingers or pierce them with a needle and there is no feeling of pain, then you understand that they are not going to recover, and in such conditions it is fatal.
And if you move those limbs and feel constriction when pressing on them and piercing them with a needle, then you understand that the vertebra is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if the vertebrae of the neck show similar symptoms and you want to know whether they will recover or not, then observe their breathing.
At this point, if you see that they are already weakened and the condition affecting them is similar to what happens in their limbs, and shortly after lying on their back, they experience vomiting and involuntary defecation, and sometimes they lie on their back and want to urinate but cannot, then you understand that it is fatal, and at that moment there is no need to work on treating them.
But if none of those symptoms occur, then the cause is better and farther from being fatal.
And the treatment for them is to apply a poultice to reduce the abscess on the affected vertebra. Place over it, at times, rose oil or boiled linseed; place this over it several times until the abscess is reduced.
During the hours, apply one of the comfortable and stimulating plasters, and place it over that area; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility and not to sleep on the side where there is pain until it is healed.
And if it happens that the bone is bruised or for some cause has already become separated from it, then place a cloth over the wound and remove that bone; and immediately adjust the two lips of the wound, using stitches if it is large; and thereafter treat it with one of the ingredients of the remedies until it is healed.
But if the tip of the bone is broken and it is the end of the limb, then place your finger of the left hand in the buttock, and align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible by second-degree reduction; and thereafter apply a plaster and splint to it, if you need those, and secure it.
And if you feel bruised and broken bones in it, then act on that, remove them, and treat the wound with the treatment that has been prescribed until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
On the restoration of the broad (area).
The bones of the broad areas are often broken, and if they break, then the fracture in them is only communication at the extremities of those bones and they split along their length and incline to the sides; and the patient experiences pain in that location and puncture, and the movement is restricted when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it aligns with the figure of the fracture.
And if the fracture is at the extremity of the limb, then you should apply padding according to what seems appropriate to you by way of equalization until it resembles its natural shape.
And if the fracture is in the long bone that inclines towards the lower parts, then you should have the patient lie on their back so that you may prepare for the restoration of that fracture. And when you have padded that, place an emplaster over it, and then place over that a splint of wood or cane, and bind with bandages in such a way that you do not fear any point of the fracture nor movement of the splint.
And pad the concavity with what fills that space until it takes the binding according to equality, and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or side.
And if an abscess occurs suddenly, then you should stop the extension of the limb and its restoration until the abscess is brought under control, and apply what soothes it as has been done previously; and then return to the restoration of the limb, and bind it as is appropriate.
And if you encounter crushed or comminuted bones, then you must treat them accordingly.
Chapter XI.
On the restoration of the fracture of the adjunct (note: "adjutori" may refer to a specific part or bone, possibly translated as "auxiliary bone" or a supporting structure, depending on the context). For precise translation, more context would be helpful, but based on the text, it refers to the restoration of a fracture involving an auxiliary or supportive bone.
The adjunct is what is between the shoulder blade and the top of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, the restoration of it is in one of two ways, of which the first is that you take smooth wood of medium thickness in this form:
And let it be held in two places by threads, and let it be suspended from a high place.
And let the patient lie on a stool; and then lay the broken arm over the wood until it reaches the midpoint where the curve of the wood is attached.
And then suspend something heavy on that, or the patient will extend it to the lower parts; and then the physician should place the fracture with its sides upwards until the fracture returns to its proper position.
And the other way is that the patient lies supine on their back and suspend the arm at the neck with a thread; then command two assistants, one to hold above the fracture with its upper part, and the other to hold below it; each should extend it in their direction.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then tie threads above and below the fracture, and each of the two assistants should pull in their respective directions.
And if the fracture is near the end of the muscle, then it is necessary to place half of the thread over the midpoint and the other part below the fracture towards the joint.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the joint, then it is necessary to place the thread over that location and above the joint.
Take that [wound or incision] with ease and work on it, and as soon as it is well healed according to what is needed, then strengthen it if an abscess does not occur.
But if an abscess does occur, then loosen the stitches internally on the seventh day, and place over it a sponge soaked in vinegar and rose water until the abscess subsides, and then you can strengthen it.
And the method of strengthening it is to apply a plaster over the fracture, and then place a bandage of wet cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm on that support, and place its hand upon its own muscle.
And place the cloth and strengthen it over the support for the arm so that the arm is in a position of stability, if there is no cause prohibiting this from the broken bone.
And now use a splint.
And it is that you place on the fracture a splint that is thicker and stronger than the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers from all sides.
And then strengthen it on the splints with the bandaging method that I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandaging over the site of the fracture should be tighter. And when the fracture is longer, the bandaging should be lighter.
And so, when you see the situation of the splints and the bandaging as we have said during the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then you should proceed accordingly.
And if you fear an abscess, apply caustic on the seventh day as we have said.
And for this reason, consider bandaging all parts for three days so that nothing happens in the place of swelling or inflammation; or food should not reach the member due to excess tension; and thus correct everything as we have said.
But if you are confident that no cause of this will occur, then do not loosen the bandage for at least three days or more.
And let the patient lie on their back, and their head should be raised to rest on their shoulders so that it is above the stomach.
And place a padding filled with wool over the helper, and at all times of the night or day ensure that the figure of the broken limb is not loosened or dissolved, nor should the bandage be too slack; thus correct this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we mentioned earlier, such that the food is light at the beginning until healing starts; and then it is appropriate to gradually increase the quantity of food.
For the usual practice regarding the splint and bandage is that they should be tight for forty days; and then it is appropriate to loosen them, administer a bath, and treat with ointments that correct this.
And if the fracture is rough and irritated, then do not loosen the bandage and splints for several days or even up to two months.
Chapter XII.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the ulna; of which one is small, and it is the one that lies under the wrist, and the other is large and is placed below the small one, towards the lower part.
And often it happens that only the larger ulna is fractured, or the smaller one, and frequently both are broken together.
And when the smaller ulna is fractured above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is quicker.
And when the ulna is fractured below, the fracture is severe, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is fractured is the smaller ulna above, then it is appropriate for the physician, during its restoration, to allow a small extension with ease until it is bandaged.
But if the larger ulna is the one that is fractured, then it is necessary to allow more vigorous extension.
And if both ulnas are broken together, then it is necessary to allow the most vigorous extension.
And it is necessary to place the figure of the hand during its restoration and extension stretched out on a cushion, with the wrist of the hand at the upper parts, as mentioned before, and the thumb be lower than the other fingers, and the patient lie resting on a soft bed.
And let a cushion be placed under him on one side or the other, so that the patient does not have to work.
And let one minister extend the limb to be operated from below by the waist or with a bandage, and another minister extend it similarly from above.
And let the surgeon then adjust the bone into place as best he can according to its proper shape.
And if in the setting of the bone you find any protrusions, then you must reduce all parts to their proper place with your skill.
And if a protrusion appears separated from the rest that prevents healing and is not to your hope for the restoration of the patient, then you should act upon it and remove that part according to the method we have described above.
But if the fracture is due to infection, I have already treated this in a separate chapter, so follow the treatment described there.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration process, then apply a cloth dipped in wax made from olive oil and white beeswax, which should be between thick and thin consistency. Place a strong bandage over it with gentle pressure to allow the abscess to subside; then remove the wax and apply a plaster prepared from powdered herbs mixed with egg whites, and subsequently place linens; let the linen placed over that fracture be farther and tighter.
And know that the number of bandages for the arm varies in major treatments, or rather when the fracture is in one bone or in two bones simultaneously.
And then apply your bandaging over the site of the fracture tightly and forcefully. When more strain is placed on the parts above or below the fracture, apply the bandaging a bit looser according to the preceding instructions at the beginning of the chapter.
The cloths with which you wrap the fracture should be soft and moist, not hard or rough, and the bandage that is applied should be of medium thickness between fine and coarse, as we have recommended.
And consider the limb and the bandaging after several days. And if any cause arises for which adjustment is necessary, such as inflammation that occurs in the limb, you should immerse the limb in hot water until the swelling subsides.
And do not constrain the limb until it rests, and then resume the bandaging.
And if the bandaging has already been loosened and the bone is in a similar condition, then adjust everything with your skill.
And be careful if the nutrition is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive bandaging, for then you should loosen it slightly and leave it for several days so that nutrition can reach it, and then tighten it.
But if the patient does not suffer from any of the causes we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to remove the bandaging for about twenty days or thereabouts.
And then suspend the arm of the patient due to their skill in moving it actively, and have them lie on their back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days or thirty-two days, or sometimes it is restored in twenty-eight days, and all of this depends on the condition of the tissues and the skill of the practitioner.
Chapter XIII.
On the restoration of fractures of the bone of the hand and the fingers.
Know that in the bones of the hand and the joints of the fingers, when there is a fracture, it rarely affects them unless after many days.
And when a fracture or dislocation occurs in the hand, it is necessary that the patient lie flat and on a hard surface, and place the hand upon it extended.
And then the assistant should extend the broken bones, and the physician should ensure that they are properly aligned.
And now it is necessary to apply a plaster and wool padding, unless an abscess requires cauterization; then place a board above according to the size of the area, and you should have wrapped that in cloth.
But if the fracture is in the parts inside the palm, then make it appear as a sphere of cloth, and instruct the patient to hold that with their injured palm; then wrap it tightly with cloth.
And the board should be made of wood and wrapped in cloth in which it is raised, so that the surface fits the palm; then wrap it as needed.
But if the fracture is in the parts outside, then place a board above and another below in the palm, so that the hand is supported and straight; then apply the bandage, as if rotating the hand at the wrist between the fingers with ligaments.
And if it happens that there is a fracture in one of the bones mentioned, then immediately the said bone should be straightened according to what is necessary and placed upon the palm, and if you wish, place under it a small straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not injured.
And if the fracture is in any of the others mentioned, such as in the middle bone, or in the index finger, or in the ring finger, then it should be straightened and placed on the affected finger; or all should be laid out in sequence, which is better; or place it over that small splint, as we have said about the thumb.
