And consider, in your restoration and subsequently, the disposition of abscesses and opposing conditions — during your operation — according to what is necessary when any cause of those occurs, as it is repeated in the narration.
Chapter XIV.
On the restoration of a fracture of the bone of the forearm.
The bone of the forearm, when fractured, is felt and revealed to the sense according to how it has moved apart and to the side.
And the restoration of it is done in such a way that one bandage is stretched over the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, and the patient lies prone on their stomach and face.
And subsequently, the minister should extend all the bandages to their position according to its quality, when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is closer to the wrist of the forearm, then the bandage should be extended towards the palm at the position of the metacarpal, so that the extension reaches there, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And if the fracture is near the knuckle, then at this time bind it to the knuckle, and extend it to the parts below.
And then the surgeon should equalize the bone on both sides but ensure that it is reduced according to its natural appearance and the bone is joined by good union; now it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs, then you should leave it for several days until the abscess subsides, and afterward return to your treatment.
The bandaging should be done as follows: you must wrap around the fracture a hard bandage, and fold it two or three times, and remove the excess; then wrap the limb so that it reaches to the ankle and the root of the thigh.
And insert a long strip of cloth between the thigh and the limb near the joint at the parts below, and let the ends of the cloth extend from the uppermost part of both sections, and then wrap around the limb and the thigh what remains of the bandage.
And then place strips of metal over the thigh at the location of the fracture, and place one strip over the bone of the limb.
And then fill the spaces between the limb and the thigh with soft cloths, so that the bandaging is compacted.
And then begin a strong bandaging over the location of the fracture with three or four wraps, and as you move away from the site of the fracture with the bandage, your wrapping should become lighter and looser and softer.
From there extend to the two extremities of the vein to the parts below until it reaches the navel cavity, and strengthen both the extremities of the stitches on the other side so that the ligature is not loosened from its place.
And then lay the structure over that, so it is such that corrosion or abscess does not occur in the member nor swelling and similar things; and if any cause of those should happen, immediately cut away what is damaged and correct it right away as we have said many times.
But if in the bone there is a piece of bone that needs to be placed, then try to align it as best you can; and if not, fix it in place and do not move it, and care for what precedes the healing until it is healed.
And at times this fracture of the rib is strengthened by stitching it to another part in the same way as we have said about the assistant and the arm.
But in the restoration of the limb in such a manner that no bad lameness occurs to the patient; and if it is restored alone or another chamber is applied to it, then it is necessary that the patient limp permanently.
And know that the rib is strengthened in forty days or a few more months, and all of this depends on the diversity of constitutions and other circumstances.
Chapter XV.
On the restoration of the fracture of the thumb bone.
Know that the fracture of the thumb bone often occurs at the base, but it happens many times near the joint.
If it happens that you encounter a fracture, at that moment it is nothing but a split or injury to its parts, and this is in one side or six sides, and on this everything stands as feeling.
And the restoration of it consists in putting together what is broken of its parts as they are inside, so that it may be added and joined according to what is possible by equation, ease, and fabrication.
And then apply a plaster and place it over that round bandage if you need one; and strengthen above the constriction as is appropriate.
And then consider all the positions which we have recommended for other fractures, as well as abscesses and similar conditions, in such a way that you oppose every accident accordingly until it is healed.
Chapter XVI.
On the restoration of the fracture of the hip.
Know that in the hip there are two bones of which one is thick and is named the hip bone; and the other is slender and is called the femur.
And if any of the types of fracture occur to these two bones as occurs to the two bones of the arm, and by this restoration is made in the same way as the restoration of the arm, and the operation for it is one.
And if you break both bones together, the hip is affected in all parts.
And if the more slender bone is broken, the hip is affected in the forward parts.
And if you cut into the bone that is thicker, feel for its surface, and be cautious of those causes which could harm you.
And then administer the extension, alignment, and binding of the bones according to that measurement.
However, be careful if the fracture of the skull is hard and uneven; then the extension should be gentle and slower, and use your utmost caution.
And when there is any cause for an operation on the skull, and when you align the bones and complete all of the work involved in the procedure, at that time you will take pieces of wood from a tree that has thick fibers under its bark; these should be made similarly to palm branches. Choose those with slight roughness, not grooved like fingers, and their length should match the length of the skull, leaving an inch inside.
And then wrap around each piece of cloth in two layers according to its length, and place one over the front part of the skull on one side, and the other on the opposite side, leaving an inch inside at the base of the head.
And then bind the two bones in three places at both ends and in the middle, for with this structure the skull is prevented from tilting to either side and remains in proper position.
And now a piece of wood has been administered according to the length of the skull; and it should be placed in such a way that the skull can still move slightly.
And it is especially important that you do this in the case of a fracture, and when it is properly diagnosed as such.
And so on each day consider the wound and study it according to that last study, and you guard it from abscess and inflammation or other similar causes; and when any such cause occurs, then act upon what is needed for it to be healed.
Chapter XVII.
On the fracture of the heel bone and of the bones of the foot and of the aforementioned.
A complete fracture does not often occur in the heel bone; but fractures sometimes happen to the bones of the foot. Few fractures occur to the aforementioned bones of the foot, and they do not usually occur there except in cases of disposition [for such injuries].
And if a fracture occurs to the bones of the foot and those bones become elevated above each other, then the patient should rest their foot on prepared ground in such a manner.
And then rest one foot on what is elevated by those bones, and then press on them, and bind them until they return to their proper place.
And then apply a plaster and place it above, and below the arch of the foot place a small board with two flat ends.
And then bind that to the foot in a proper position, since you have wrapped it with cloths and for other necessary reasons.
And when three or four days have passed, then you loosen the bandage, for you will find the bones bound together, or joined, or dislocated, and the operation on these is as you know.
But if any of them are joined together, then separate them and bind them as I have recommended to you in the restoration of those of the hand. Take a piece of wood and shape it according to the length of the finger, and make it slightly thicker at one end; then place this tool below this table as recommended to you, and tighten it with appropriate firmness.
If what is being cut involves two, three, or more bones, then place over each finger a stick of its quantity wrapped in linen cloth, and tighten the table at the base of the foot, so that it stands on one end of the table, thereby providing good support for the arch of the foot.
It is important for you not to forget that you should consider all the causes mentioned to you in the other fractures and accidents that we have discussed; and prepare yourself for every possible situation as needed.
Chapter XVIII.
On the fracture of the chin in women and of the pubic bone and penis in men.
When the chin of a woman is fractured, then make that suture in the form of a crescent, and slightly incline it towards one side, and let it be placed behind.
Then the midwife should fill the chin with cotton, and it should be placed in the chin as if it were a sphere; then gently encourage the woman to cough, and gradually elevate her head, for all of this is intended so that before long the chin will return to its proper position as if it were a sphere and the fractured bone will heal.
Then place pillows under her head, and when she needs to urinate, lift the cotton for ease and allow her to urinate, then reduce it and return it according to the method by which you initially placed it, and then return it to its original pillow.
And so it should be done for about seven days or around that time, because then it is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and stretch it over the wound with a tube of cane; and insert the entire bladder into the cavity, and then blow air through the tube until the bladder is inflated inside the cavity, because the fracture returns to its place.
And then fill it with pitch and keep it for several days as we have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the thumb of a man or woman is broken, then administer what we have recommended for the hip bone in its restoration and alignment.
And what is right is not hidden in these nearby fractures that rarely occur, especially to one who has some study and use of understanding my book; for this fracture signifies to you in the major positions through directness in its restoration and constriction, and thus you understand well.
But in the arm of a man when it is broken, take a splint made of wood, and place the arm in that; then cover it with a cloth wrap, and bind it, and leave it for three days or around that time, and it will be healed.
Chapter XIX.
On the restoration of bone fractures when there is inflammation.
It is appropriate for one who suffers a fracture with inflammation, and especially if the bone is large such as the thigh bone or the helper bone, or similar to that, that you apply cupping and bloodletting immediately if the conditions for bloodletting indicate it to you as we have said above.
And if the pus flows from the wound, now is the time for you to turn your attention to the retention of the powder that you triturate and sprinkle on the wound, unless some other cause is present.
And immediately begin to restore the fracture on that very day, and do not delay it, unless an abscess occurs.
But if an abscess occurs, now you should postpone the restoration for seven days until the abscess subsides, and do not approach it on the third day or even the fourth, for you may cause further harm.
But if the broken bone protrudes above the skin, now is the time for you to reduce it and equalize it with your utmost ease and with minimal extension.
But if you are not prepared for extension and cannot equalize it with your utmost effort, now you should reduce it using this instrument; the instrument is made of iron, its length being seven or eight digits, and its width according to the quantity of the wound; and therefore, it is necessary for the physician to take three or four such instruments according to what is needed for the treatment in all types of fractures.
And let it be round, and let it be in slight convexity so that it does not become bent under the pressure upon it during the operation.
And let it have sharp edges at its ends, with margins on both sides; and let the upper part be thicker in tendency, and from the middle to the parts thinner.
And this is the form of it:
And it is called in Greek "voluntary palate flap", that is a small palette.
So that the extremity of the bandage may be laid flat, raise it above the extremity of the prominent bone.
And impel it into a place where the bone can return to its proper position and become aligned with some fixation.
And thus strive to align the ends of the fractured bones between them, and if the end of the broken bone is slender and does not take the instrument well, then you should insert the end of that bone so that the instrument has grip on it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone as we have recommended, then insert it with that which seems appropriate to you among the instruments we have mentioned; or cut it with one of the saws in whatever manner is possible for you.
And rasp that which remains on the bone of roughness and small ridges.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient feels intense pain in the tooth, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and thus do what will provide great relief to the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is complete, then soak a cloth in styptic black vinegar, and if it is summer, do not place wax or any other oily substance on the wound, so that decay and corruption do not occur.
And afterwards administer poultices at the appropriate time of your bone restoration, and leave the wound uncovered so that you can insert dressings with forceps and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
And guard yourself with all caution that you do not touch the plague on the fracture, for many times fools among physicians have done this to their patients, causing death or corrupting illness, which is gangrene. And if the swelling is soft, lax, different from the swelling of other fractures.
And if the swelling is large or it is bad swelling, and you fear some of the harmful accidents which we have recounted, and you feel pain in that place, then do not rest on those splints, but make an envelope of hard cloths instead of splints and apply them to it.
And as soon as one or two days have passed and the swelling begins to subside, indicating that pus has formed inside, then you remove the cloth which you had placed over it, and soon administer poultices, such as those we customarily use for treating swellings, such as tetrafarmakon and similar remedies.
And it is necessary for you to loosen the bandage and examine the swelling every morning and evening until it is consolidated and firm, and it should be such that the pus can flow from it to the lower parts with ease.
If several days pass and the swelling does not consolidate or pus does not begin to discharge from it, then you should know that here one must use small incisions to inquire about the condition of the swelling with a probe. And of those incisions, those that are separate and can be explored and extracted, you should do so. For those from which nothing can be removed and which cause pain when the limb is moved, perforate them as much as possible according to what is feasible for you.
And if it happens that the wound is gangrenous or ulcerated, which are stinking and other kinds of corruption and putrefaction, then you should use one of those remedies with which it is convenient for the treatment of which a preceding narration is given in its chapter.
And concerning that in which my speech must stand and you should present your procedure: when the bone is broken, it protrudes above the limb as does the bone of the wing and the helper and similar to those of large limbs, and do not assume traction of it, nor the extraction of it for whenever more than two weeks or three passes it causes death; therefore leave that inside which heals, for surely light it will fall out in about twenty days or thirty; and then care for the wound if you see fit, and if not, leave it as is.
Chapter XX.
On the treatment of knotting that occurs in the aftermath of some fracture.
This knotting often occurs in the aftermath of the healing of a fracture, especially near the joints, and it disfigures the shape of the limb; and it significantly impedes the limb from its natural function.
Then consider whether the knotting is recent and administer those medicines that have astringent properties such as aloes, myrrh, mastic, sarcocollo, atractylis, and similar ones; take some or all of these and combine them with vinegar or egg white or water to make a paste. Apply this paste over the knotting using a piece of linen, and secure it firmly in place with proper bandaging.
And leave the bandaging in place without removing it for several days; then remove it and apply another, until the knotting is reduced. Or gradually heat a lead plate over it, as lead has the property of reducing what is swollen in limbs.
If a swelling has become hardened and turned into bone and it is narrow and causes necessity to remove it quickly, then cut it along the part where pus is most abundant, and excise the excess that protrudes or make an incision in such places as are convenient for it to be removed; and take care within so that it may heal.
Chapter XXI.
On the restoration of fractures when they are set and then the limb is moved gently but according to its natural movement.
When a fracture is set and the limb is moved gently, it is done for various reasons: one of which is too much soaking of the bandages and binding them not as needed; or by excessive tightness of the bindings within which the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb; or by an excess of poultices; or by unnecessary movement out of its proper time; or by a lack of blood in the patient's body and weakness.
And the treatment for this is to give food to the patient and make their body soft so that blood is multiplied in it; administer baths; and apply warm herbs and myrtle over it.
And then apply a plaster to the limb so that the plaster draws in nourishment, or continue poulticing it with warm water until the nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter XXII.
On the treatment of broken bones when they are restored incorrectly and prevent the necessary operation on them.
When it happens to the member that has already been restored near its healing from curvature and prominence of the broken bone and joint stiffness, and this is because of the form of the limb, but nevertheless the limb is not prevented from its natural function, then there is no need for you to accept the word of one who believes that limb to be newly broken.
But now there are many false doctors and restorers who have done this in our land; and this operation is a shameful one leading to great fear, the sign of which is fatal.
But if limping and stiffness are recent, then they should be soaked in hot water with soft herbs such as willow bark, hawthorn leaves, and melilot; and a soothing plaster should be applied like well-made mallow; or take mucilage of marshmallow root and mix it with linseed oil or olive oil, then apply it; or soak fatty figs in pigeon dung and use similar remedies for strengthening the limbs.
And in some cases, it is dissolved through frequent gentle friction, which should be applied to the most moist areas, and movement of the limb should be administered to all parts at all times.
But if the curvature has already advanced and is hard and turned into bone and necessitates treatment with iron, then it must be that the pus above should be evacuated and the continuity of the bone should be dissolved, and that which is excessive in stiffness of that member near the bone should be cut with fine instruments, and ease should be administered to it with diligence and study.
Chapter XXIII.
On Speaking of Dislocation.
Dislocation is when any of the joints loses its proper location, thus preventing movement, and the figure of the limb becomes distorted, causing severe pain and great injury.
When a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to immediately attempt to reduce it and not delay in doing so, for if there is a delay, the joint becomes inflamed and swollen, making the reduction of the dislocation difficult.
And for this reason, it is not advisable to move or keep the patient standing during the period of inflammation, as movement often causes spasms and severe pain.
But when this happens, it is necessary for you to prepare for the incision of the affected area, and then allow time for the inflammation to subside; afterward, the limb should be bathed in warm water and oil, and then reduction should be attempted with ease.
And treat the entire limb with what will help it remember its proper place.
And thus I have arranged the chapters on dislocation as they were previously organized for fractures; starting with the more important parts of the body and then proceeding to the lesser ones.
Chapter XXIV.
On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Lower Jaw.
When both jaws are dislocated, they do not move easily; the dislocation of both can occur in one of two ways: either they are partially displaced from their proper positions with some movement and slightly softened; or they are completely and fully dislocated, to the point where they are softened at the joint where saliva flows from the mouth and the patient cannot close it properly, and the tongue is covered by the soft tissue.
When the dislocation of the bone is slight, it returns by itself in major positions due to slight cause.
If the dislocation is complete and severe, it must be treated with immediate reduction, and should not be delayed at all.
And it is that the minister have the patient lie down, and insert his fingers into one side of the mouth towards the site of the dislocation if the dislocation is on one side; or insert both sides with his fingers together if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers should be outside equally; and he commands the patient to cease moving their jaws and relax them so that they can move from each part; and the physician then aligns the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated simultaneously, then administer steam with hot water and oil until the reduction can be performed gently on both sides, and do not delay the reduction of both from entirely as we have said.
And when they return and are properly aligned, and the patient's mouth is closed and not relaxed, then place soft cotton pads covered with wax over them, and make it from beeswax and oil softened; and afterwards bind it gently with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head be positioned between both ears, so that it does not turn to either the right or left; and do not force them to chew for any reason; have their food be cut into small pieces, until when the pain subsides and the jaws are well healed, then allow them to chew as they wish.
Thus it should be administered with ease, and he should not work on the opening of the mouth for eating and drinking and speaking until the jaw is healed and cured.
And if the reduction of both jaws when they are dislocated at any time and have not returned to their places is difficult, fevers often occur with constant sweats, and very easily the patient's belly becomes distended, and very easily he vomits pure bile.
And when you see this, then you know it is lost; and very often he who suffers this dies within ten days.
Chapter XXV.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the muscle.
The collarbone is not dislocated from the chest internally due to its continuity with the ribs; and sometimes it is dislocated outward, and this is evident by sensation.
And the restoration of it is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. And then press the area with your palm firmly, for it will return.
And then apply a plaster and powder to that area.
But the extremity of that which connects to the neck and is continuous with it is not dislocated on any day; therefore, it must be reduced and treated as we have said and as you are prepared.
And then apply a plaster, powder, and bandaging to that area, and instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And in this method of treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter XXVI.
On the Reduction of Muscle Dislocation.
Know that a muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, one of which is when it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
The second is when it is dislocated at the upper part of the muscle, and this happens quickly; and it is not dislocated to the rear parts by the shoulder; nor is it dislocated to the front parts by the nerves.
It is more often dislocated and comes out below the upper part, especially in those whose flesh is thin, it dislocates easily; but in those with thick muscles, it comes out with difficulty.
It often happens to some people that due to a blow or fall, their muscle becomes swollen and inflamed, and it is caused to be dislocated by this swelling.
Then you should understand this while you are in charge of it, and at that moment proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation is known when it is below the upper part; thus, you can distinguish between a dislocated muscle and one that is not, as there is a clear difference between them. You will find a concavity at the top of the muscle below the upper part upon touching its head as if it were hollow, and the affected person cannot move their hand to their ear or perform any other movement with it.
If it is dislocated, you should place the foot above, and you will immediately recognize this clearly, and it is not hidden.
And from this dislocation a quick reduction is made when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the surgeon raises the limb to the upper parts; then he places his hands below the joint and raises it firmly back into its place with strength, and the surgeon raises the hand of the patient, and extends it as needed; then he pauses it there until it returns quickly.
But if it does not return as we have said, and at the beginning when the dislocation occurred, after a few days, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water and given embrocation to soften and relax it as if for treating rheumatism, using alfalfa, fenugreek, and melilot in water.
And then the patient should be placed on their back with their joint hanging down to wait for wool that will fit between the laxity and tightness. Then the physician places the patient's heel on a ball and raises the muscle upwards vigorously, and pulls the patient’s hand so it is not injured below, while another assistant holds the patient's head to prevent it from being hurt below, as it quickly returns to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this manner: have a man present who is taller than the patient, and let him stand on that side. Place the patient's muscle below the joint and raise the joint upwards so that the patient is suspended in the air, while another assistant pulls the patient’s hand from below their abdomen. If the patient is restless, it is necessary to keep him occupied with something else or divert his attention for some other reason, because otherwise the dislocation will recur immediately.
And it is reduced in another way: Place a long post in the ground with its top rounded like the pestle of a mortar, neither too thick nor too thin, and have it positioned near the patient; then place linen cloths over the top of the post. The patient should stand along the length of the post, and extend one arm behind the post while the other side of the body is extended with strength, so that the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if the reduction of it is difficult due to all the causes we have mentioned, then use this treatment: Take a post that is two arms in length and four fingers in width, and its thickness should be proportionate to the size of a reed according to this form:
And then place linen cloths over the round top so that the post does not hurt the patient.
And then place the reed near the patient's armpit, and extend the arm or forearm over the post towards the opposite side, and wrap the post with helpers and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then place the arm on a step of a ladder according to its width, and extend the hand towards the opposite side, while the other side of the body is suspended from the other side, because the joint will return immediately.
And when the instrument is completed according to whichever method is possible for you, then place a pad of wool under the armpit of equal quantity; afterward, apply a plaster prepared with powdered moly and egg white over the entire muscle, as mentioned above, and then bind tightly around the armpit with proper tension. The bandage should be wrapped around the plaster, and the patient's arm should be attached to their neck, and left alone without disturbing it for seven days.
When the patient's food has been restricted for a short time, until the limb is strengthened, for it is easier to heal thus.
And when the food is given more freely after seven or five days and movement is exercised; and thus if it is firm and not softened, then it is healed.
If the joint is dislocated due to repeated exposure to moisture that occurs to it or for some other reason, then cautery should be administered three times, as was said before in the chapter about cauteries.
And if all this is done and after seven days the bandage is removed and the joint is not stable, and the plaster and compression are reapplied several times, yet it remains unstable and the patient cannot raise the arm above certain points, then you should know that the nerves of it, which attach to the head of the bone, have been cut or stretched or softened, and understand that the joint will never be fixed in its proper place.
Regarding the dislocation that occurs from the feet and the thigh or the parts behind, the reduction is performed with impulse and extension, until it returns to its proper position.
And let the other compression be applied to it, and care for it until it is healed.
And if heaviness and slowness in movement occur during the healing process in the limb, then the patient should use baths several times until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its original nature.
Chapter XXVII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the collarbone.
Know that the joint of the collarbone is dislocated with difficulty and returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, but mostly forward or backward. The dislocation does not hide from you when it lies below the view or touch according to its figure, and when you adjust the dislocated bone, this is clearly declared to you because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm nor touch that muscle.
And you must treat it, and reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before it becomes inflamed, for if it becomes inflamed, it is difficult to reduce it, and even when reduced, it does not heal completely; especially when it is in the backward position, because it is worse than all other types of dislocations, more painful, and many die from this.
And the restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the hand be extended towards the two shoulders, and the arm be expanded, and the doctor's hand should be above the collarbone and below it, and he presses the joint with his fingers together, or with the palm of his hand until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the forward direction, then it returns as he bends his hand towards himself, until he strikes the collarbone with the palm of his hand.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer vigorous extension and force.
And it is that two assistants extend the arm, and hold it firmly by both sides so that it does not move due to the extension.
And so wrap the arm completely, because it is wrapped in a long doubled cloth around the hand, or with bandages.
And when the surgeon touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that this helps with the lubrication of the joint and ease; then forcefully push the joint inward until it returns.
And so for the reduction of the shoulder, it should be placed on a support that has stiffness and stability with an eagle's claw bandage, and it should be wrapped tightly with strictures downwards, and the arm should be suspended from the neck, and it should be left like this for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and if the joint is formed in its place, now you untie the bandage of it and leave it like that.
And if you see that the joint is not properly set, now you should reapply the plaster and bandages, and leave it like that for several days until it is healed; then loosen it.
But if you encounter serious pain during the reduction of the joint and the patient is in motion, now administer hydration through bathing and gentle friction, and compression until it is relieved; or place something soothing on the joint, as movement causes discomfort; then bandage it, and leave it on the bone day and night. And then take that off, and put the patient in a bath; when they are dry, now rub the joint with moderate friction, and then reapply something soothing on it, as movement causes discomfort, once, and the second time, and the third time. take an instrument for probing, enter with it carefully.
And if you wish, place a poultice over that moist and inflamed fissure at the edge and apply it; do this for several applications, since that area is moist, and reduce it to its proper condition.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the wrist, which is the joint between the arm and the hand.
When the wrist of the hand is dislocated in some cases, the reduction of this dislocation is easy, but the opposite is true for other joints. However, it is important to act with speed in reducing it at the moment when it is dislocated, before swelling occurs or an abscess forms.
And the reduction of this dislocation is done by placing the joint of the patient's hand on a table and having an assistant extend the patient’s arm. The physician then places his hand or palm over the prominence of the joint and presses it until it returns to its proper place.
However, it is important to consider whether the palm is already displaced toward the front of the hand. If so, the patient should place the two sides of their hand on a table during extension and reduction; if the dislocation is toward the back of the hand, then the position of the patient's hand should be placed directly on the table, so that the physician’s hand can press over that prominence of the joint.
And if it returns to its place immediately, that is good; but if not, then apply a compress over the swelling and leave it like that; do not repeat this, for otherwise, neither will it tolerate nor can you perform the reduction of the dislocation beyond a few days.
However, if the joint remains rigid according to its curvature; and you find no other cause for injury, except that the hand is softened and cannot grasp any cause; then you know that the nerves are severed or torn, and then there is no generation in it unless it be strengthened by cauterizing proper to the case and thus confer some cause.
Then when the inflammation has subsided, you should pause on the bandage which we have recommended, and let it lie and strengthen for five days; and thereafter it should be loosened and left, and the hand of the patient should be exercised.
However, if the movement of the patient remains restricted and you encounter some gravity in it, then treat that with warm water and friction several times until it is relieved.
Chapter XXIX.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the said joint.
In some cases, the said joints are dislocated completely.
And when any of those fingers is dislocated toward the thumb or the palm, then hold the finger and apply pressure to its dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
And then support the tip of the finger, and suspend it above the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
And then suspend it and extend it until it is properly aligned on its proper day.
And when it is night, bandage it as described, and do not cease to loosen it during the day and exercise with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for several days until it becomes strong.
If it seems necessary, if the joint is dislocated at the elbow, and bind that to that part and do with this the first operation until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated at other parts.
Chapter XXX.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the spinal vertebrae.
When a complete dislocation occurs to any of the spinal vertebrae or neck vertebrae and the vertebrae move out of their places, there is no treatment at that time, for death is feared for the patient.
The sign of this is that the excretion of the patient occurs without their will, and they cannot control it; often some parts of the body on one or both sides are weakened, or an arm or leg becomes paralyzed; or a vertebra has moved from its place, as sometimes it moves and at other times it returns to its original position.
When that has moved to the rear parts, it is called kyphosis.
However, the treatment for this is that at that time you must consider whether the kyphosis occurred due to weakness, because in such a case there is no treatment and no complete recovery.
However, in the case where it occurs due to falling or impact or similar causes, the Ancients described the methods of treatment at length, which I have summarized for brevity; but I have simplified that from which little is lost by my proximity to understanding and explanation, with which I have expounded and formulated using a different approach than that used to demonstrate this and explain that.
And those are not cured except for the one that occurs between the two precisely as I mention.
And it is that the patient lies on his back on a flat surface near a wall, and he should be placed low enough so that the feet do not touch the ground.
And near a straight pole should be placed, planted in the ground at the end of the bed around his head, and another pole on the other side by his feet at the other end of the bed.
And the attendant should hold the pole, and it should not be fixed in a hole; and another attendant should hold the other according to that form.
And wrap a bandage around the head of the patient and under the armpits tightly bind it; and extend the end of the bandage to the pole that is behind his head, and tie it there; and then stretch the other bandage over the hips of the patient near the buttocks; and then adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other pole that is behind his feet.
And then the attendant should extend the bandage to the side of the pole, and do not move the two poles and the bandages from their places where they are placed, but rather keep them as we have said.
And the physician should place his palms on the patient's spine with strength until he returns, or place him on a table, and then adjust the patient’s feet on the table until he returns.
If the patient does not return in this manner, take a table whose width or length is about three arms' lengths, and dig near where we said the patient should be a place into which the end of the table can fit. And place the middle of the table over the spine, and the physician places his weight at the other end, and gently press until the spine is compressed and returns to its proper place.
And if you want, open again the part that is wrapped around the hand; and place the patient's head at the end of two posts, the length of which should be one arm's length, and behind the patient's head place two more posts in the same manner; and the distance between each post should be a palm, and secure each post firmly to the ground so that it does not move.
And place one round post in which is the ring, and let it be wrapped around the two trunks of the two posts.
And at the end of it is a trunk from which the post is formed, the length of which is one palm around where it is wrapped; and on the two other posts there is something similar to that.
And then tighten the ropes that you have tied to the patient's feet at the trunk behind his head, and the ropes that you have tied around his shoulders at the ring behind his legs.
And then place someone behind each ring who will turn the ring with their hand, and the physician will compress the spine as we have said.
And after the spine is returned and the place leveled, now cover with an astringent plaster mixed with egg white, and then apply wool; and then place over the plaster a stick of wood whose width is three fingers or thereabouts, and whose length should match the extent of the swelling, and on either side of the spine.
And then wrap it with the bandage we have mentioned, and use a nutritious but light diet until it is healed.
And if there is any stiffness in the area at the end of the healing process, now it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and lubricate the joint over a long period, as we have recommended; and also apply a lead plate to that location.
And sometimes stiffness occurs in the back of the lower spine due to that dislocation; and it is this bone for which one should not make any preparation with this medicine, for it could easily cause death.
Chapter XXXI.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the attachment of the muscle are subject only to dislocation, and do not suffer what other joints with small movement and concavity may endure.
And the joint of the hip can be dislocated in four ways: either inward, outward, forward, or backward.
And it is more seriously dislocated towards the interior parts, and slightly towards the anterior or posterior parts.
The sign of the dislocation of the thigh towards the interior parts is that when you adjust the bed of the healthy limb against the affected one, it appears longer and its joint is more prominent than on the healthy side. The patient cannot bend their leg towards their chin, as this position has become inflamed due to cauterization for an abscessed condition; therefore, the head of the bone has already reached this point.
The sign of the one to whom a dislocation occurs towards the exterior parts is that the symptoms are opposite to those mentioned in this context.
The sign of the one to whom a dislocation occurs towards the anterior parts is that when the bed is elevated according to the complement, they cannot bend it without pain in the joint, and if they attempt to walk, they cannot do so towards the front. The urine of the patient is retained and their swallowing is affected by an abscess, and they are unable to walk on the toes.
The sign of the one to whom a dislocation occurs towards the posterior parts is that they cannot extend the joint, nor can they bend it forward as much as they could before, and their bed appears shorter than usual. The swallowing (or throat) is softened, and the head of the bone is displaced in that location.
However, the reduction of these types of dislocations is that you consider whether the dislocation is old and ancient. If it has already become fixed in place, you administer reduction according to its disposition at that time, but it may not be fully cured then; therefore, no complete assurance can be given about the outcome.
In the case where the dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four types of dislocation, place your hands so that you can turn the joint and extend it to its limits both inwardly and outwardly, and move it to the right and left, for it easily returns to its proper position and does not require other treatment.
If it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare a strong instrument and extend the ball of the joint from its posterior part, or with its fingers, or with a bandage which is laid over the upper bone; and another person should extend the hand above in such a way that their fingers are placed behind the tubercles. Then tightly bind a lion's skin over the region of the joint, and have another person hold the extremity of the bandage.
And this extension should be from the side or the inner part toward the clavicle, or from the posterior part toward the spine.
And perform all of these extensions at once while the patient is raised onto his shoulders and kept hanging.
And this type of extension is common to all four types.
And so if the dislocation returns as we have said, it is good; and if not, then for every type what I say about its proper treatment is necessary.
In the reduction of its own proper place when it is dislocated, and if the dislocation is toward the inner side, have the patient lie supine on their back; then place bandages over the region of the joint in that location where between the head of the joint and the place below the clavicle; then extend the bandage from above the armpit to the farthest part of the clavicle.
Take another strong minister and grasp his arms, and firmly hold the place of the joint that is dislocated and extend it to its parts outward with great extension, for that returns it to its proper place.
And this species is the quickest to heal among those in which this member is reduced compared to the others.
But if he avoids you and does not obey the treatment for this species of cure entirely, then it is necessary to bind both sides of the injured part together with a strong, quick, and firm bandage over both heels and both toes.
And let the length of each be equal to four fingers in quantity, and let the injured hand be extended more than the other by a quantity of two fingers.
And let the injured person be suspended over the top of the beam, and hold it with his hand; and let the length from the ground to him be equal to two arms.
And then command a strong young man to pull the head of the joint, and let another young man hold the injured one, while the other young man pulls the joint with great strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction, when it is a dislocation to the outward part, let the injured person lie on his back as we have recommended for lying down after eating; and bind the bandage firmly over the injured hand properly and over his feet.
And then place two beams, one under his feet and the other under his head; and then place adjusted beams on each side of the shoulder placed upon which moist cloths are wrapped, so that the injured person is not harmed by the beam being between his arms, to prevent the extension downward parts from being pulled.
Let each attendant extend his part, and the surgeon with his skill completes the reduction according to art.
And when the dislocation is to the anterior parts, then it is necessary that the limb be extended quickly, and according to this narration on the back; and the surgeon places the palm of his right hand on the affected area, and with both hands pulls it in such a way that the extended part goes from below to the front.
And if the dislocation is to the posterior parts, then it is necessary that the patient be extended to the side and raised off the ground; however, it must be placed on a hard surface, as it should also be done if the hip is dislocated outward, according to what we have said about positioning on two boards; and it is on his back with the limbs extended, as we have said, there is no harm.
And it is necessary to administer compression on the table at the location of the joint.
Thus, when the reduction is complete as required — and the sign of hip reduction is not hidden from you, which is that you extend both legs of the patient, and when you see them equal and the patient extends his own leg and spreads it for comfort, then you know that the limb has returned as required — and then you adjust two things and apply a bandage so that the hip is not injured in any part; and instruct the patient to rest for three or four days.
Then loosen the bandage and the plaster, and compare one cavity with the other.
Now, if you see those equal signs in the garment, now you know that the dislocation has already been fixed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any sign of softening, now you should return and apply a plaster to it, and strengthen it according to your original structure, and let it be for three days.
Then loosen that [area], and have the patient delay in walking on each [limb] for several days, until it is strengthened.
Chapter XXXII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it can be dislocated outward, inward, and backward toward the back; but it cannot be dislocated forward.
And the sign of knee dislocation is that you command the patient to apply their knee to the chest; now you know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all forms of dislocation is that you have the patient lie on their side, such that they can extend their leg as much as possible; place your hand against the side of it, and put the foot between your legs; then adhere the knee with your palms, and hold them firmly over the knee with your hands, and another assistant should straighten the leg until the knee returns to its proper place.
And the sign of recovery for him is that his belly adheres to his sides without laboriousness.
And then apply a plaster to that and make the belly adhere to the sides, and then bind those areas with a bandage for three days or four, and then loosen them.
And do not use the method of walking for several days until he is healed and comfortable.
But if the reduction of him to you is unnecessary, now administer a strong extension with bandages, as was described in the preceding narration about the treatment of the hip.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the thigh.
The thigh sometimes is displaced by slight movement, and sometimes it is dislocated completely, and the dislocation of it is done either towards the interior parts or towards the exterior parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it is that you see the thigh swollen towards the part to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its displacement is easy recovery, and it should be extended with ease using the hands until it returns to place.
But in the treatment when it is completely dislocated, you must perform the procedure correctly, and let him have a strong and capable assistant from behind to help at the proper place.
And then place your right hand under the most prominent part of the thigh, and your left hand below the knee at the location of the hip joint.
Place the foot towards you with your right hand, and grasp it with your left hand for two grasps, then extend it with the left hand; and press with your feet the foot with your right hand while working.
And do this twice as we have counted.
And then press your feet on the foot for the third grasp, and you will be at the knot.
Therefore, if it returns in one grasp or two according to this method, and if the foot becomes numb, it is good; and otherwise, now repeat the operation, for that will return.
But if it is forbidden for you to reduce the grasp as we have recommended, then place the patient on their back on the ground, and tie a stick firmly to the ground, which should be positioned under the arch of the foot, and you should wrap a cloth around it so that it does not slip.
And then the attendant should hold the patient's limbs, and another attendant should extend the foot with his hands or with bandages around the neck of the foot; and then each attendant should pull against the extension according to their position, and the stick should be placed between the patient’s limbs to prevent it from being pulled towards the sides during the extension.
And then the physician should numb the dislocation with his hands, and another attendant should hold the healthy limb to the sides, as the dislocation will return with ease.
And when the dislocation returns and the health of the reduction is clear to you, now apply a plaster and wrap it firmly with bandages, and bind the foot with bandages to the leg.
Be careful not to damage the nerves which are behind the heel, so that the ligature placed over them does not cause excessive harm.
Bind it like this for two days or three; and if the ligature has softened, remove it.
And then untie it on the third day or the fourth, and prohibit the patient from moving for forty days. But if the patient is forced to move before that time, there is no guarantee that the dislocation will not be dissolved and corrupted, and after this it will not receive treatment.
And if it happens that the abscess needs to be cauterized, you should then administer according to the guidance of the liquid in which our narration proceeds, in another place for treatment and application, until it is healed.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the treatment of dislocation of the said feet.
Make sure that the person who is dislocated rests with some extension without work; and this is not difficult, but rather easy.
But if the dislocation is in some bones of either foot, then you should have the patient lie on their back on a flat surface on the ground or on a table, and they should remain lying down as if they were walking.
And then you stand, and place your foot on the area you intend to treat, and then step down gently until it returns to its normal position, ensuring that it appears flat and there is no prominence.
After the pause, place the foot on a table that can support the entire weight of it, which should have two ends, and then bind it with a proper and firm bandage for three days; after this, loosen that and let it rest by walking around for fourteen days, until it is strengthened and its improvement is assured.
Chapter xxxv.
About the kinds of dislocations that are made by plague or fracture or any other circumstances.
When one of those causes occurs and you proceed with the treatment of it and the restoration of the patient, many times death follows.
And because of this, one should not presume to take charge of the treatment unless they are a wise practitioner with long experience, who is merciful and pious, not hasty or presumptuous; and at the beginning, use medicines that only reduce swellings, and leave the patient's fate to divine judgment, but refer them to someone in whom you have hope for salvation from their peril or relief from their illness, where there appears any hope. And then strive to perform the reduction for them as soon as possible at the beginning of the cause, before it becomes a swollen abscess.
And then if the limb returns as we want it to, immediately administer a regimen that reduces swelling and treats the injury with what is appropriate from among the dried remedies.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and occurs in the separated bones, then you should treat them accordingly, and do as we have said regarding simple injuries, dealing with each location as has been described.
And guard and avoid according to your virtue the one of instrument in the ways of fear as it is preceded by my preaching; for this is better for you to please your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and most salutary for your true body you will see these in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis perfectly, for which God be blessed. Amen.
